Jerusalem Talmud Taanit
תלמוד ירושלמי תענית
The Jerusalem Talmud, translation and commentary by Heinrich W. Guggenheimer. Berlin, De Gruyter, 1999-2015
https://www.nli.org.il/he/books/NNL_ALEPH001901012/NLI

Jerusalem Talmud Taanit



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> From when does one mention the Power of rains<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In the second benediction of the <i>Amidah</i> which celebrates God’s power in resurrecting the dead, during the rainy season in the winter one inserts the formula mentioned later celebrating God’s power to bring or withhold rain.</i>? Rebbi Eliezer says, from the first holiday of Tabernacles, but Rebbi Joshua says, from the concluding holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The last holiday is the eighth day of Tabernacles which follows the seven days of dwelling in huts when one returns to dwell in houses.</i>. Rebbi Joshua said, since rain during Tabernacles is not a sign<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Greek σημεῑον.</i> of blessing<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This also is Maimonides’s version. In the Babli “a sign of curse”, since rain (in Israel) during Tabernacles is a sign that Heaven is not interested in the Jews sitting in huts.</i>, why should he mention? Rebbi Eliezer said to him, he only says “He Who makes the wind blow and brings down the rain”, in its appropriate time. They answered him, if that is so, one should always mention it<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">If the intent only is to refer to the winter months, the prayer could be said 12 months a year.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “From when does one mention the Power of rains,” etc. The reason of Rebbi Eliezer, because these four kinds<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The 4 kinds of plants to be waved on Tabernacles, cf. <i>Sukkah</i> Chapters 3–4. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 2b" href="/Taanit.2b">Babli 2b</a>.</i> grow on water, they become attorneys<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Greek παράκλητος.</i> for water. Another explanation, when the slave serves all his master’s needs he asks for his reward from him. Rebbi Joshua said to him, is it not that when the slave has served all his master’s needs and his master is satisfied with him<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, by showing through the absence of rain that the service of the Jews is agreeable to Heaven.</i> that he asks his [sustenance]<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s text, overwriting the scribe’s which probably was פרס, “reward”.</i> from him? Another explanation. The slave asks for his reward only close to the time when his reward is due<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In this version, R. Joshua would have preferred to start mentioning rain only at the presumed beginning of the rainy season.</i>. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 2b" href="/Taanit.2b">Babli 2b</a>.</i>: Rebbi Eliezer says, from the time one takes the <i>lulav</i>; Rebbi Joshua says, from the time he puts it down. In the opinion of the latter Tanna, the opinion of Rebbi Eliezer in the first is his opinion in the latter<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The earlier opinion is that given in the Mishnah, the latter that of the <i>baraita.</i></i>. The argument of Rebbi Joshua is inverted. There, he says from the concluding holiday, but here he says, from the time he puts it down<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah he designates the eighth day of Tabernacles; his opinion in the <i>baraita</i> can be read as meaning that on the seventh day after morning prayers, when the <i>lulav</i> is no longer needed, one should start mentioning rain in his prayers.</i>. Rebbi Mana says, because the entire day is qualified to take the <i>lulav</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">While weaving the <i>lulav</i> during morning prayers is preferred, the obligation can be discharged during the entire day. As a generally applicable rule, R. Joshua’s statement in the <i>baraita</i> also refers to the eighth day.</i>. Should he not mention in the evening<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since the first prayer on the eighth day is the evening prayer, there seems to be no reason for R. Joshua to wait until the second prayer in the morning, which is the third after the start of the day.</i>? Not everybody is there. Then he should mention it in the morning. He would think that maybe they mentioned in the evening and he will mention. Since he sees that they do not mention in the first {prayer} but mention in the later {prayer}, he understands that they did not mention in the evening<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Joshua it would have been better to start mentioning rain in the evening prayer but this turns out to be not practicable.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Marius said to him, so was your father Rebbi Jonah arguing it.
Rebbi Ḥaggai in the name of Rebbi Pedat: It is forbidden for the individual to mention before the public reader mentions<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">At a place where there is public worship with a quorum of ten adults, no individual may recite his <i>Musaf-Amidah</i> before the reader in the synagogue starts. This version may be read to mean that the individual may not start mentioning rain unless he could have heard the mention in the repetition of the prayer by the reader.</i>. Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Is the matter dependent on the public reader<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">He seems to hold that private prayers are not dependent on public worship even in places where organized synagogue worship is held regularly.</i>? Rebbi Mana said before Rebbi Ḥaggai, do they disagree? He said to him: no. That which you are saying, is the matter dependent on the public reader? The individual if he wants to mention, he mentions dew<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">On the first day of Passover, when one stops mentioning rain after the morning prayers, every individual is free to mention dew in the benediction celebrating God’s power but not obliged to do it (in the Galilean rite.) This conforms to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> which requires that one stop mentioning rain on Passover but does not require starting to mention dew.</i>. But what you are saying is: it is forbidden for the individual to mention before the public reader mentions rain<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In the fall, where <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> requires that the mention of rain be initiated by the public reader of the second <i>Amidah</i> of the last holiday of Tabernacles.</i>. If they rise for prayer it is as if the public reader mentioned it<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The public is required to mention rain in their silent prayer whether or not the reader notified them.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following paragraphs are reproduced in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 5:2:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.5.2.2-8"><i>Berakhot</i>5:2 (Notes 63–86</a>); the origin is here. The theme is that God has to be praised for rain in the prayer for resurrection of the dead during the rainy season (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Berakhot 5:2" href="/Mishnah_Berakhot.5.2">Mishnah <i>Berakhot</i>5:2, Note 62</a>).</i> Just as the resurrection of the dead brings life to the world, so rains bring life to the world. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba understood it from here: <i>He will resurrect us after two days, on the third day He will raise us up and we shall live before Him. We shall know, we shall pursue to know the Eternal, like morning His appearance is well based</i><sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Hosea 6:2-3" href="/Hosea.6.2-3"><i>Hos</i>. 6:2–3</a>. The first verse of Hosea clearly speaks of resurrection, the second verse ends: <i>it</i>(knowing God) <i>will come like rain to us, like late rains which pour on the Land.</i> The verses are further discussed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 11:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.11.6.3"><i>Sanhedrin</i>11:8 (Note 117</a>), Babli <i>Rosh Hashshanah</i>31a, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 97a" href="/Sanhedrin.97a"><i>Sanhedrin</i>97a</a>.</i>.
It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 17:1" href="/I_Kings.17.1"><i>1K</i>. 17:1</a>.</i> <i>Elijah the Tisbite, from the inhabitants of Gilead, said to Aḥab: By the Living Eternal One, the God of Israel, before Whom I stood, there will not be dew nor rain these years except by my word.</i> Rebbi Berekhia said, Rebbi Yose and the rabbis. One of them said, he was heard both for dew and for rain; the other one said, he was heard for rain but was not heard for dew<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Berakhot</i> the two statements were added by the corrector. Since these are the essence of the argument in the entire paragraph, the original omission in <i>Berakhot</i> shows that the text there is not original.</i>, from<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>1K</i>. 18:1. But dew is not mentioned. Since the sequel is a justification for the inclusion of praise for giving dew, it shows that including the sentence “Who brings down dew” in the prayer for resurrection is Galilean usage (unknown in Babylonia), as noted by I. Elbogen (<i>Der jüdische Gottesdienst in seiner geschichtlichen Entwicklung</i>, Chapter 2, §9).</i>: <i>Go, appear before Aḥab and I shall give rain on the face of the earth</i>. He who said, he was heard both for dew and for rain, where was the vow of dew dissolved? Rebbi Tanḥum from Edrei said, they are of the opinion that a vow that was dissolved partially is dissolved totally<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">A minority opinion of R. Aqiba (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 9:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.9.6.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i>9:6</a>) that if in an earthly court part of a vow is annulled (because is was made under false intent), the entire vow is annulled. This is not the majority opinion and is not practice; therefore it does not imply Heavenly standards.</i>. Some want to say, on the occasion of the son of the woman from Sarepta: <i>He called on the Eternal and said, o Eternal, my God, also the widow with whom I am dwelling You are doing evil to kill her son</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 17:20" href="/I_Kings.17.20"><i>1K</i>. 17:20</a>.</i>. Rebbi Judah ben Pazi said, about one who stole a doctor’s bag<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Greek νάρθηξ, a plant (<i>Ferula communis</i>), word also used for “casket for unguents”. Hesiod has Prometheus carry off the fire in the stalk of a νάρθηξ.</i>. When he left, his son was injured. He returned to him and said: Please, sir doctor, heal my son. He said to him: Go and return my bag because it is full of medicines, and I shall heal your son. So the Holy One, praise to Him, said to Elijah: Go and lift the vow of dew because the dead are resurrected only by dew, then I shall resurrect the son of the Sareptan. And from where that the dead will live only by dew? <sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 26:19" href="/Isaiah.26.19"><i>Is</i>. 26:19</a>.</i><i>Your dead will live, the corpses will arise. Wake up and jubilate, those who dwell in dust! For a dew of light is Your dew</i>, וָאָרֶ֭ץ רְפָאִ֥ים תַּפִּֽיל. What means וָאָרֶ֭ץ רְפָאִ֥ים תַּפִּֽיל? Rebbi Tanḥum from Edrei said, “the earth will give up what is deposited in it.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">R. Tanḥum from Edrei explains the difficult phrase, <i>it will fell Netherworlds</i>, by reading תַּפִּֽיל in the sense of Mishnaic Hebrew “having a miscarriage”: The Netherworld will expel what is in its belly. (Read totally differently in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 111a" href="/Ketubot.111a">Babli, <i>Ketubot</i>111a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 111b" href="/Ketubot.111b">111b</a>).</i>”
<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph explains the reasons of those who say that Elijah’s prediction that there would be no dew never came true.</i> Rebbi Jacob from Kefar Ḥanan in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: When Abraham their forefather did My will, I swore to him that I never shall remove dew from his descendants forever; what is the reason? <i>The Eternal swore and He will not change His intention, you are appointed forever</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 110:4" href="/Psalms.110.4"><i>Ps</i>. 110:4</a>.</i>. And it is written there<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 110:3" href="/Psalms.110.3"><i>Ps</i>. 110:3</a>.</i>, <i>you have the dew of your youth</i>. Rebbi Judah ben Pazi said, by a will<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Greek διαθήκη.</i> I gave it to Abraham: <i>God may give you from the dew of Heaven</i><sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 27:28" href="/Genesis.27.28"><i>Gen</i>. 27:28</a>. While this is Isaac’s blessing for Jacob, later (28:4) it is called “Abraham’s blessing”.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman said, when Israel comes to sin by their evil deeds the rains are arrested. They bring an old man like Rebbi Yose the Galilean, he prays for them and immediately the rains come. But dew does not descend by the merit of any creature, what is the reason? <i>Which do not listen to anybody nor wait for humans</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Micah 5:6" href="/Micah.5.6"><i>Micah</i>5:6</a>. The verse starts, <i>like dew from the Eternal</i>, <i>like light showers on grass.</i></i>.
Rebbi Ze`ira in the name if Rebbi Ḥanina: When someone stood {praying} in the rainy season and mentioned dew one does not make him repeat. In the season of dew and he mentioned rain one makes him repeat. But is it not stated<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 3b" href="/Taanit.3b">Babli 3b</a>.</i>: The Sages did not oblige one to mention dew and winds but if he wants to mention them, he may mention? One cannot compare one who takes it easy<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">And does not mention the dew which he needs, but mentions the rain out of season.</i> to one who does not pray and does not take it easy<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Who prays neither for dew, nor for rain, nor for winds, and therefore does not slight anything.</i>. “When he stood in the rainy season and mentioned dew one does not make him repeat.” But is it not stated: If he did not ask for it in the benediction “for years”<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The ninth benediction where in winter one prays for rain, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 5:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.5.2.1">Mishnah <i>Berakhot</i>5:2</a>.</i>, or he did not mention the power of rains in “resurrection of the dead”, one makes him repeat? That is about one who mentioned neither dew nor rain.
Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rav Ḥuna: If he did not ask in the benediction “for years” he says it in “He Who hears prayer”. If he did not mention the power of rains in “resurrection of the dead” he says it in “He Who hears prayer”. If the request, made under duress<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The request for rain is made only in the rainy season, “under duress” when rain is urgently needed. The mention of rain in the second benediction already starts “under easy circumstances” around the time of the fall equinox, some weeks before rain is needed. The benediction “He Who hears prayer” is the last of the middle benedictions, #15 (in Babylonia #16) on weekdays.</i>, may be recited in “He Who hears prayer,” the mention, made under easy circumstances, so much more! But is it not stated: If he did not ask in the benediction “for years” or that he did not mention the power of rains in “resurrection of the dead”, one makes him repeat from the start<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">As a tannatic statement, this has precedence over the statement of Rav Ḥuna. How can Rav Ḥuna present his statement about power of rains? [The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 29a" href="/Berakhot.29a">Babli, <i>Berakhot</i>29a</a>, has the statement formulated here in the name of Rav Assi from the Yeshivah of Rav in Babylonia; the Babli mentions neither the statement of Rav Ḥuna, Rav’s student, nor the explanation of Rebbi Abbamari.]</i>? Rebbi Abbamari<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Brother of the Amora R. Yose of the fourth generation. In the <i>Berakhot</i> text, the statement is attributed to R. Eudaimon, another brother of R. Yose. Neither of the two brothers is mentioned in the Babli.</i> the brother of Rebbi Yose, said, if he did not say it in “He Who hears prayer”!
Where does he return to<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">If he forgot either prayer for or mention of rain, and realizes his omission at the end of the <i>Amidah</i>.</i>? Just as Rebbi Simeon bar Abba said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: On the day of the New Moon<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">When the day has to be mentioned in “Temple service”, the first of the last three benedictions; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 4:1:27" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.4.1.27"><i>Berakhot</i>4:1 Note 90</a>.</i>, if he had moved his feet<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">During <i>Amidah</i> it is forbidden to move one’s feet since the angels do not move their feet (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:18" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.18"><i>Berakhot</i>1:1, Note 61</a>). Hence, moving after prayer is the outward sign that prayer is finished.</i> he repeats from the start, otherwise from “Temple service”. Here also, if he had moved his feet he repeats from the start<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Otherwise, he returns to “He Who hears prayer.”</i>.
At Nineveh<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">This is not Biblical Niniveh but Naveh in the center of the Bashan plateau, see <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:1:19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.1.19"><i>Demay</i>2:1 (Note 99</a>). (Eusebius mentions that the Jews call it Nineveh) On the Golan plain, one needs spring rains if the winter was relatively dry.</i> they needed to make a fastday after Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 14b" href="/Taanit.14b">Babli 14b</a>.</i>. They came to ask Rebbi. He told them, go ahead and make one but do not change the form of prayer<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Since if one fasts for rain one must pray for rain in one’s <i>Amidah</i>.</i>. Where may he say it? Rebbi Jeremiah thought to say that he says it in “He Who hears prayer.” Rebbi Yose said to him: Did not Rebbi Ze`ira say in the name of Rav Ḥuna: If he did not ask in the benediction “for years”, or that he failed to mention the power of rains in “resurrection of the dead”, he says it at “He Who hears prayer”? But Rebbi said to them, go ahead and make one but do not change the form of prayer! According to the opinion of Rebbi Yose, where does one say it? In the six that he adds<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Six additional benedictions that the reader inserts in the public prayer of fast days for rain, as explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.2.1">Mishnah 2:2–10</a>. Rebbi Yose implies that even a local fast for rain is a public fast.</i>. That works for the public which has six. A private person who does not have six, where? Rebbi Ḥinena said, did not Rebbi Ze`ira say in the name of Rav Ḥuna<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 4:4:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.4.4.9"><i>Berakhot</i>4:4 Note 212</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 31a" href="/Taanit.31a">Babli 31a</a>.</i>, a private person asks for his personal needs in “He Who hears prayer,” and these are his needs<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Here ends the parallel in <i>Berakhot</i>. In the last two paragraphs, the omissions in the scribe’s text of <i>Berakhot</i>, filled by the corrector as given in the readings, again show the originality of the text in <i>Ta`aniot</i>.</i>.
It was stated: If he prayed but does not know what he mentioned, Rebbi Joḥanan said, all of thirty days there is a presumption that what he is used to he (would mention) [mentioned]<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">There is no difference in meaning between the texts of the (scribe) and the [corrector], even though the scribe’s text seems more appropriate. Since it was stated in the paragraph before the last that the <i>Amidah</i> has to be repeated in whole or in part if the appropriate mention of or prayer for rain was omitted, the question arises what to do if the person does not know whether he recited the required text or not. Within thirty days one presumes that he has to repeat, after thirty days one presumes that he has not to repeat. Since prayers and prayer texts are purely rabbinic institutions, this must be accepted as rabbinic decree. The problem is not mentioned in the Babli; therefore it is accepted as universal practice.</i>. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rebbi Ḥuna: On the second holiday of the diaspora he mentions in the morning prayers but not in the <i>musaf</i> prayer<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 4b" href="/Taanit.4b">Babli 4b</a> this is quoted as opinion of the Galilee trained Rav (a little more in detail, that on the 8<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> day of Tabernacles one mentions rain only for <i>musaf</i> and on the 9<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> one starts with <i>musaf</i> and from there on one continues in all prayers), but rejected by the autochthonous Samuel.</i>. Afternoon prayers like morning prayers and evening prayers like <i>musaf</i> prayers. Ḥananiah the son of Rebbi Joshua’s brother<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">His name, as was his father’s, was Ḥananiah ben Ḥananiah; two generations of posthumous sons. One avoids the mention of this unlucky circumstance by circumlocution. He was one of Samuel’s ancestors.</i> says, in the Diaspora<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">This may mean either Babylonian in general or Nahardea, presumed residence of King Jojachin, in particular. From the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 10a" href="/Taanit.10a">Babli 10a</a> one understands that all of Iraq is included, from Nisibis to the Persian gulf.</i> they did not have this custom, but until sixty days after equinox. Samuel said, practice follows Ḥananiah the son of Rebbi Joshua’s brother<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 10a" href="/Taanit.10a">Babli 10a</a>.</i>. And it was stated thus: When was this said? Outside of the Land. But in the Land of Israel, everything according to the season, everything according to the event<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The standard commentators emend the text, switching the places of inside and outside the Land. This switch is unjustified. It is stated that in Babylonia uniformly one starts praying for rain on the 20<sup>th</sup> of November, 60 days after the fall equinox, but in the Land of Israel the different dates for starting to pray for rain given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.3.1">Mishnah 4</a> are matters of local need or local usage (denied in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 10a" href="/Taanit.10a">Babli 10a</a>).</i>. That is, about rain. But dew, if he wants to mention the entire year he may mention it.
“Rebbi Joshua said, since rain during Tabernacles is not a sign<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Greek σημεῑον.</i> of blessing<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This also is Maimonides’s version. In the Babli “a sign of curse”, since rain (in Israel) during Tabernacles is a sign that Heaven is not interested in the Jews sitting in huts.</i>, why should he mention? Rebbi Eliezer said to him, he only says ‘He Who makes the wind blow and brings down the rain’, in its appropriate time.” In its time it is desirable like the resurrection of the dead. Rebbi Yose said, I am wondering how the rabbis could compare rainfall to the resurrection of the dead, but they are not comparable. A person prays all the time for the dead to become alive. Does a person pray for rain to fall all the time<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Since the last sentence of the last Mishnah in this Chapter notes that in Palestine summer rain is a curse.</i>?
“They said to him, if that is so, one should always mention it<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the Mishnah, not quite correct. It is the objection of R. Eliezer to R. Joshua’s statement.</i>.” The argument of Rebbi Eliezer seems inverted; the argument of Rebbi Joshua seems inverted<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is not about the prayer text of the <i>Amidah</i> but about the doxology of the introductory benediction preceding to <i>Amidah</i>, “praise to You, Eternal, Redeemer of Israel”. According to R. Eliezer, redemption uniquely depends on Israel’s actions; therefore prayer for redemption either is unnecessary because all of Israel are repenting or it is useless because not all of Israel are repenting; the prayer text should be forbidden as vain invocation of the Name. According to R. Joshua the prayer is legitimate but one cannot understand why during the summer one should not mention God’s power to give rain in its time just as one prays for redemption in its time.</i>. As they disagree: Rebbi Eliezer says, unless Israel repent they never will be redeemed, as it is said, <i>by repentance and satisfaction you will be helped</i><sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 30:15" href="/Isaiah.30.15"><i>Is</i>. 30:15</a>. The interpretation agrees with the interpretation in Targum Jonathan ben Uziel.</i>. Rebbi Joshua said to him, if Israel would stay and never repent, would they never be redeemed? Rebbi Eliezer said to him, the Holy One, praise to Him, causes a hard king like Haman to rule over them and immediately they repent and are redeemed. What is the reason? <i>It is an evil time for Jacob and from it he will be helped</i><sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 30:7" href="/Jeremiah.30.7"><i>Jer</i>. 30:7</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joshua said to him, but is it not written, <i>gratis you were sold and not by money you will be redeemed</i><sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 52:3" href="/Isaiah.52.3"><i>Is</i>. 52:3</a>. Not by money, but by repentance. The argument is switched between RR. Eliezer and Joshua in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 97b" href="/Sanhedrin.97b">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i>97b</a>.</i>? How does Rebbi Eliezer deal with this? Repentance, as you are saying, <i>a bundle of money he took in his hand</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 7:20" href="/Proverbs.7.20"><i>Prov.</i>7:20</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 96b" href="/Sanhedrin.96b">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i>96b</a>, the “bundle of money” is interpreted as referring to the pious people (whom God took away by death, since in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 7" href="/Proverbs.7"><i>Prov.</i> Chapter 7</a> the adulteress is interpreted as apostle of apostasy.) If this interpretation is accepted here, R. Eliezer’s argument is that for redemption it is not required that all of Israel rise to the status of pious people, but universal repentance is required nevertheless</i>, etc. Rebbi Joshua said to him, but is it not written, <i>in its time I shall quickly do it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 60:22" href="/Isaiah.60.22"><i>Is</i>. 60:22</a>.</i>. How does Rebbi Eliezer deal with this? Repentance, as you are saying, <i>but now, Israel, what does the Eternal, your God, ask from you but to fear</i><sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 10:12" href="/Deuteronomy.10.12"><i>Deut</i>. 10:12</a>. Fear of God is repentance.</i> etc. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: If you merit it, <i>I shall quickly do it</i>; if not, <i>in its time</i><sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 98a" href="/Sanhedrin.98a">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i>98a</a>.</i>. When Rebbi Joshua said to him, <i>he lifted his right and his left arms to heaven and swore by the Eternally Living that in term, terms and a half, when he finished to smash the hand of the holy people, all this will finish</i><sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Daniel 12:7" href="/Daniel.12.7"><i>Dan</i>. 12:7</a>. In the entire talmudic literature it is understood that the prophecies contained in Daniel have no relation with Maccabean times.</i>, Rebbi Eliezer withdrew<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Since this prophecy is unconditional. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 98a" href="/Sanhedrin.98a">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i>98a</a>.</i>.
Israel were redeemed from Egypt for five reasons. [Because of the term,]<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, to be deleted.</i> because of tribulation, because of entreaty, because of the merit of the Fathers, because of repentance, because of the term<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The predetermined date for their rescue, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 15:13" href="/Genesis.15.13"><i>Gen</i>. 15:13</a>.</i>. That is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 2:23-25" href="/Exodus.2.23-25"><i>Ex</i>. 2:23–25</a>.</i>, <i>it was in these important times that the Children of Israel were sighing at the work and crying,</i> because of tribulation; <i>and God heard their wailing</i>, because of entreaty; <i>and God remembered His Covenant,</i> because of the merit of the Fathers. <i>And God saw the children of Israel,</i> because of repentance<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 2:25" href="/Exodus.2.25">Pseudo-Jonathan (<i>Ex</i>. 2:25</a>) reads <i>saw</i> and <i>knew</i> as parallels, matters that only God knew or saw: The Eternal saw the pain of the servitude of the Children of Israel and their individual repentance was evident before Him, since no one knew of his neighbor’s.</i>, <i>and God knew,</i> because of the term. And so it says<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 4:30-31" href="/Deuteronomy.4.30-31"><i>Deut</i>. 4:30–31</a>.</i>, <i>if you are in trouble,</i> because of tribulation; <i>and all these matters will find you in future times and you will repent</i>, because of repentance; <i>for the Eternal, your God, is a merciful power</i>, because of mercy; <i>He will not let you sink nor destroy you, nor will He forget your Fathers’ Covenant</i>, because of the merit of the Fathers. And so it says<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 106:44-46" href="/Psalms.106.44-46"><i>Ps</i>. 106:44–46</a>.</i>, <i>He saw how they were in trouble,</i> because of tribulation; <i>when He heard their prayer</i>, because of entreaty; <i>and remembered for them His Covenant</i>, because of merit of the Fathers; <i>and consoled them in the abundance of His grace</i>, because of repentance; <i>and He had mercy with them</i>, because of mercy.
A person sinned with his tongue<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">He was used to gossip about other people, which is sinful behavior.</i>. He came before Rebbi Joḥanan who sent him before Rebbi Ḥanina. The latter<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">His instructions for repentance of gossipers are a matter of controversy between his sons in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Arakhin 15b" href="/Arakhin.15b">Babli <i>Arakhin</i>15b</a>. </i> told him, go, examine yourself, and study Torah, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 15:4" href="/Proverbs.15.4"><i>Prov</i>. 15:4</a>.</i>, <i>the tree of life</i><sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Which customarily is identified with the Torah (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:18" href="/Proverbs.3.18"><i>Prov</i>. 3:18</a>.)</i> <i>is healing for the tongue.</i>
Rebbi Ḥanina ben Rebbi Abbahu said, in the book of Rebbi Meïr they found: <i>a saying about Duma</i>, “a saying about Domae<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Reading רוֹמֵי ”Romae”.</i><i>, to me He called from Seïr</i><sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 21:11" href="/Isaiah.21.11"><i>Is</i>. 21:11</a>. Since <i>Seïr</i> is the place of Edom, and Edom is the code name for Rome, the prophecy about Duma is taken as a prophecy about Rome. This paragraph is the introduction to the next one, referring to the second half of the verse, reassuring the people that the night coming from Seïr (the oppression by Rome) will come to an end.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, He called to me because of Seïr<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the oppression by Rome, God is calling to His people even without their repentance.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, from where was He inimical to me, from Seïr<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">This is the opposite of R. Joḥanan’s interpretation. Because of the bad behavior of the People, Rome is an organ of divine retribution.</i>. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, if a person asks you, where is your God? Tell him, in the great fortification<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Since in his time, at the start of the military anarchy, Rome was not fortified, the reference must be to the seat of the Roman government. Greek χάραξ “palisades”.</i> at Rome. What is the reason? <i>To me He called from Seïr</i><sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">If this is the introduction to the next paragraph it means that in his time the most important Jewish diaspora was in Rome.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 29a" href="/Taanit.29a">Babli 29a</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Bo</i> Chap. 14; <i>Sifry Num.</i>84, 161; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shemot Rabbah 15:17" href="/Shemot_Rabbah.15.17"><i>Ex. rabba</i>15(17</a>).</i> Rebbi Simeon ben Yoḥay stated: Every place to which Israel was exiled, the Divine Presence was exiled with them. They were exiled to Egypt and the Divine Presence was exiled with them. What is the reason? <sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 2:27" href="/I_Samuel.2.27"><i>1S</i>. 2:27</a>.</i><i>Was I not revealed to your Father’s house when they were in Egypt, the house of Pharaoh?</i> They were exiled to Babylon and the Divine Presence was exiled with them. What is the reason? <sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 43:14" href="/Isaiah.43.14"><i>Is</i>. 43:14</a>.</i><i>On your behalf I was sent to Babylon</i>. They were exiled to Media and the Divine Presence was exiled with them. What is the reason? <i>I installed My throne in Elam</i><sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 49:38" href="/Jeremiah.49.38"><i>Jer.</i>49:38</a>.</i>, but Elam is nothing else but Media as you are saying, <i>when I was in the Capital city of Susa which is in the province of Elam</i><sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Daniel 8:2" href="/Daniel.8.2"><i>Dan</i>. 8:2</a>. The identification is erroneous.</i>. They were exiled to Ionia and the Divine Presence was exiled with them. What is the reason? <i>I shall awake your sons, Zion, over your sons, Ionia</i><sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Zechariah 9:13" href="/Zechariah.9.13"><i>Sach</i>. 9:13</a>.</i>. They were exiled to Rome and the Divine Presence was exiled with them. What is the reason? <sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 21:11" href="/Isaiah.21.11"><i>Is</i>. 21:11</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 21:12" href="/Isaiah.21.12">12</a>. The previous paragraph is the introduction to this sermon.</i><i>He calls to me from Se`ir: watchman, what time is it in the night?</i> Israel said to Isaiah: our teacher Isaiah, what will lead us out from this night? He told them, wait until it will be asked. After he asked, he returned to them and they asked him, <i>watchman, what time is it in the night?</i> He said to them, <i>the watchman said, there comes morning and also night.</i> They said to him, <i>and also night</i>? He told them, not as you understand it. But “morning” for the just ones, and “night” for the evildoers. “Morning” for Israel and “night” for the peoples of the world. They asked him, when? He said to them, any time you want, He wants: <i>if you desire to ask, ask</i>. They asked him, what obstructs? He told them, repentance: <i>repent, come.</i>
<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Slightly differently and in the name of R. Levi <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shir HaShirim Rabbah 5:2" href="/Shir_HaShirim_Rabbah.5.2"><i>Cant. rabba</i>5(2</a>); cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 98a" href="/Sanhedrin.98a">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i>98a</a>.</i> Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Tanḥum ben Rebbi Ḥiyya: If Israel would repent on one day, David’s son would immediately come. What is the reason? <i>Today</i>, <i>if you will listen to His voice</i><sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 95:7" href="/Psalms.95.7"><i>Ps.</i>95:7</a>.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Midrash Ps.</i>95[3]; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 118b" href="/Shabbat.118b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>118b</a>.</i> Rebbi Levi said, if Israel would keep one Sabbath following its rules, David’s son would immediately come. What is the reason? <i>Moses said, eat it today, for Sabbath is this day to the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 16:25" href="/Exodus.16.25"><i>Ex</i>. 16:25</a>.</i>, etc. One day. And it says, <i>by repentance and satisfaction you will be helped</i><sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 30:15" href="/Isaiah.30.15"><i>Is</i>. 30:15</a>. The interpretation agrees with the interpretation in Targum Jonathan ben Uziel.</i>. By keeping the Sabbath and staying put you will be freed.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One asks for rain<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the mention of God’s power to bring rain (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>), which is recited in the part of the Amidah which is pure praise and recited daily, prayer asking for rain is different and part only of the weekday <i>Amidah</i>.</i> only close to the rainy period. Rebbi Jehudah says, the one who stands before the Ark on the last holiday of Tabernacles<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">The Eighth day which is a separate holiday, where one does not sit in the <i>sukkah</i> and would not be importuned by rain. Since this is a holiday where nothing should be demanded from God, R. Jehudah must refer to the praise of God’s power to bring rain, not prayer asking for rain.</i>, the last one<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">The reader of the <i>musaf</i> service.</i> mentions but the first one<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">The reader of the morning service.</i> does not mention. On the first day of Passover, the first one mentions but the last one does not mention.<br>Until when does one pray<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.2.1">Note 101</a>, R. Jehudah prescribes stopping the praise for rain at the start of the Passover festival; in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> he does not indicate a time for stopping the prayer.</i>? Rebbi Jehudah says, until Passover has passed; Rebbi Meïr says, until Nisan has passed, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joel 2:23" href="/Joel.2.23"><i>Joel</i>2:23</a>. Since it may happen that all winter rains are concentrated in Nisan, one should not stop praying in Nisan even though late spring rain is very infrequent in Israel.</i>, <i>He will bring down for you rain, early rain and late rain, in the first {month}.</i>
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Joḥanan said, practice follows Rebbi Jehudah who said in the name of Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Jehudah’s statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> contradicts his statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.2.1">Mishnah 3</a>, only one of them may be followed in practice. R. Joḥanan notes that the one which will be determined not to represent R. Jehudah’s personal opinion will be actual practice.</i>. Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The reason of Rebbi Jehudah, that all holidays end with dew<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Since in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> R. Jehudah holds that in all Passover prayers except the first two one mentions dew but not rain in the second benediction of the <i>Amidah</i>, R. Abun’s statement must refer to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>, since in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.2.1">Mishnah 3</a> R. Jehudah requires a mention of rain in all Passover prayers. This conforms to actual practice.</i> since dew is a good sign<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Greek σημεῑον.</i> for the world.
The argument of Rebbi Jehudah seems inverted. There<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>.</i> he says, “the one who stands before the Ark on the last holiday of Tabernacles, the last one mentions but the first one does not mention;” and here<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.2.1">Mishnah 3</a>.</i> he says so? One {Mishnah} in his own name, the other one in the name of Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra. But we do not know which one in his own name and which one in the name of Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra. Since “Rebbi Joḥanan said, practice follows Rebbi Jehudah who said in the name of Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra, and Rebbi Abun said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The reason of Rebbi Jehudah, that all holidays end with dew since dew is a good sign for the world,” this implies that the first one is in his own name, the second one in the name of Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">“First” here must refer to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.2.1">Mishnah 3</a>, “second” to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>. Only <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> is practice. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 4b" href="/Taanit.4b">Babli 4b</a>.</i>.
They asked before him<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan.</i>, or might we say, here to mention and there to ask for<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">One stops mentioning rain on the first day of Passover but continues to pray for rain all intermediate days of the holiday.</i>? He said to them, practice is that where one mentions one asks<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">If one stops the praise for rain one also has to stop the prayer. From the following it is clear that for R. Joḥanan and his students this also applies to fall; one has to start praying for rain immediately in the night following the last day of Tabernacles. Accepted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 4b" href="/Taanit.4b">Babli 4b</a> for dwellers in the Holy Land.</i>. Rebbi Yose said to Rebbi Ḥanania the brother<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">From the following it is obvious that one has to read “son of Rav Hoshaia’s brother” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 1:2:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.1.2.7"><i>Šabbat</i>1, Note 180</a>).</i> of Rav Hoshaia, do you remember when we were standing in front of your uncle Rav Hoshaia’s store, Rebbi Ze`ira passed by and we asked him, but he said, this<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">The date when best to start praying for rain.</i> is still problematic for me; Rebbi Yasa passed by and we asked him, but he said, this is still problematic for me. But at the end you heard from him: there is no difference; where one mentions one asks. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba came from Tyre and said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, practice is that where one mentions one asks. Rebbi Aḥa preached in the house of study, Rebbi Jeremiah preached in the council synagogue, practice is that where one mentions one asks. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.3.1">Mishnah 4</a>.</i>, “[on the third of]<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">The corrector’s addition reproduces the start of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.3.1">Mishnah 4</a>. It is possible that the scribe’s text correctly gives the argument. The exact date of the start of the prayer for rain is in dispute, but all opinions concur that one only starts in the second month of the year, whereas R. Joḥanan requires starting during the first month, in the evening prayers of Tishre 23.</i> Marḥeshwan one asks for rain”? Rebbi Tanḥum bar Ḥiyya said, that was stated in Temple times<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">In Temple times one had to let people return safely from Jerusalem to their homes in Egypt, Palestine, or Syria. But after the destruction of the Temple everybody stayed at home and one is able to combine mention and prayer also in the fall.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">This is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 6:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.6.2.6"><i>Šeqalim</i>6:2, Notes 87–88</a>.</i> It was stated: Rebbi Jehudah said, in the present world grain grows in six months and trees produce every twelve months, but in the future grain will grow in a month and trees will produce in two months. Rebbi Yose said, in the present world grain grows in six months and trees produce every twelve months, but in the future grain will grow in fifteen days and trees will produce in a month, for so we find that grain grew in the days of Joel in fifteen days, and the ‘<i>Omer</i> was brought from it. What is the reason? <i>The people of Zion shall enjoy and be happy in the Eternal, your God, for He in truth gave you strong rain, early rain and late rain in the first {month}</i><sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joel 2:23" href="/Joel.2.23">Joel 2:23</a></i>. How does Rebbi Yose satisfy Rebbi Jehudah’s reason, <i>in its months it shall produce first fruits</i>? Every month it shall produce first fruits.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> On the Third of Marḥeshwan<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The eighth Month of the year, counting from Nisan.</i> one prays for rain. Rabban Gamliel says on the Seventh of the month, fifteen days after Tabernacles, so that the last of the Jews may reach the river Euphrates<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Then he is in Mesopotamia where rains start only at the end of November; he is not hindered by rains falling near the coast of the Meditteranean.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The Mishnah is Rebbi Meïr’s, as it was stated, <sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 6a" href="/Taanit.6a">Babli 6a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 63a" href="/Nedarim.63a"><i>Nedarim</i>63a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Taanit (Lieberman) 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Taanit_(Lieberman).1.3">Tosephta 1:3</a>.</i>“what is the time of rainfall? Rebbi Meïr says, the early one on the Third, the average one on the Seventh, and the late one on the Seventeenth. Rebbi Jehudah says, the early one on the Seventh, the average on on the Seventeenth, and the late one on the Twenty-third. Rebbi Yose said, the early one on the Seventeenth, the average one on the Twenty-third, and the late one on the Thirtieth day<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The 30th day after the first of Marḥeshwan, which may be the first of Kislev.</i>, since we find that particular people<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The community leaders start to fast in a year of drought; Mishnah 5. In the Tosephta this is formulated as a statement of R. Yose, contradicting Mishnah 5.</i> only start to fast on the New Moon of Kislev.” Rebbi Abbamari, the brother of Rebbi Yose, said: everybody agrees that the Seventeenth is the time for the pleiads to disappear<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">Since the pleiads are visible only in summer. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 59a" href="/Berakhot.59a">Babli <i>Berakhot</i>59a</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rosh Hashanah 11b" href="/Rosh_Hashanah.11b">Babli <i>Roš Haššanah</i>11b</a>.</i>, since on it the Deluge came over the world. What is the reason? <i>On this day all sources of the deep abyss were opened</i><sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 7:11" href="/Genesis.7.11"><i>Gen</i>. 7:11</a>. In the verse, the date is “the 17th day of the Second Month.”</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Zamina, Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: Practice follows him who says, “on the Third of Marḥeshwan one prays for rain”<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Denied in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 6a" href="/Taanit.6a">Babli, 6a</a>.</i>. And why did he not say, following Rebbi Meïr? There are Tannaim who switch<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">The tradition of names here, in the Tosephta and the Babli is not beyond dispute.</i>.
How<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">The text almost to the end of the Halakhah also appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 9:2:20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.9.2.20"><i>Berakhot</i>9:3 (Notes 155–162</a>, <b>ב</b>) and partially in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 13:16" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.13.16"><i>Gen. rabba</i>13(16</a>).</i> much rain should fall that a man is required to recite a benediction? Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: At the start<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">If in a year of drought a public fast was proclaimed and before the start of the fast it rained, everybody agrees that the fast is cancelled if the rain has agricultural use. (This is the only criterion known to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 25b" href="/Taanit.25b">Babli, 25b</a>.) If one started to fast, one interrupts the fast according to one of the criteria “at the end”.</i>, that it should fertilize, at the end that a brick should be soaked<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">That a brick should become soft from the rain. The Arabic dictionary translates קרמיד by “(fired) brick, roof tile; plaster of Paris.”</i>. Rebbi Yannai ben Rebbi Ismael in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: At the start, that it should fertilize, at the end that the seal of the amphora should be soaked. Is the seal ever soaked<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Are these statements only phrases which hide the fact that a fast for rain which has started is never interrupted? The fast will be interrupted for a real downpour.</i>? But it is as if it were soaked. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rav Jehudah, Rebbi Jonah, Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel: At the start, that it should fertilize, at the end even a minimal amount. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Ze`ira: It was said for the interruption of fasting<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the question was asked about the benediction required by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 9:5:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.9.5.13">Mishnah <i>Berakhot</i>9:3</a>, the statements are about aborting a fast announced in a drought. The implication is that the benediction, or alternative prayer as described in the next paragraph, is required if the amount of initial rainfall is of agricultural significance.</i>. Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Naḥum, and Rav Ada bar (Ikoma) &lt;abimi&gt;<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">The name &lt;used in &lt;i&gt;Berakhot</i>&gt; is to be adopted here also. were sitting. Rebbi Naḥum said to Rav Ada bar (Ikoma) &lt;abimi&gt;: Is it not reasonable that this was said for the benediction; he answered: Yes. <sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Added from <b>ב</b>, needed but missing here because of homeoteleuton.</i>&lt;rebbi Ḥizqiah said to rav ada bar (ikoma) &lt;abimi&gt;: Is it not reasonable that this was said for the interruption of fasting; he answered: Yes.&gt; He said to him, why did you say “yes” here? He said to him, because he follows the argumentation of his teacher. Rebbi Mana asked Rebbi Ḥizqiah, who is his teacher? He said to him, Rebbi Ze`ira. He said to him, so we are saying: Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Ze`ira: It was said for the interruption of fasting.
Rav Jehudah bar Ezechiel<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">He is Rav Jehudah, frequently mentioned in both Talmudim.</i> said, so was my father Ezechiel used to recite for rainfall: May Your Name be magnified, sanctified, praised, and elevated, our King, for every drop that You bring down to us, and you make them refrain one from the other<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Differently in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 6b" href="/Taanit.6b">Babli 6b</a>.</i>. [As it is said,] <i>for He reduces water drippings; they combine as rain for a flood</i><sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 36:27" href="/Job.36.27"><i>Job</i>36:27</a>.</i>, as one says, <i>the valuation will be reduced</i><sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:18" href="/Leviticus.27.18"><i>Lev.</i>27:18</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yudan, the father of Rebbi Mattaniah, said, not only that, but He brings them down in measure, as it is said: <i>Water He determined by measure</i><sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 28:25" href="/Job.28.25"><i>Job</i>28:25</a>.</i>.” Rebbi Yose bar Jacob went to visit Rebbi Yudan from Migdal. While he was there, rain started to come down and he heard his voice saying: Thousands of thousands we are obliged to thank Your Name, our King, for every drop that You bring down to us, for You do good things for the guilty ones. He said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose bar Jacob to R. Yudan from Magdala.</i>: From where do you have this? He said to him: This is the benediction that Rebbi Simon recited for rainfall.
How much rain should fall that it is considered fertilizing<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">The root רבע of רְבִיעָה may mean either “to lie down”, as an Aramaism parallel to Hebrew רבץ, or it may have the same sexual connotation as the synonym שכב. Therefore רְבִיעָה either may be interpreted as the rain which “lies down” on the earth and moistens it for agricultural use or as “fertilizing”.<br>There are two parallel traditions, both of Galilean origin. The text here is almost identical with <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Taanit (Lieberman) 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Taanit_(Lieberman).1.4">Tosephta 1:4</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 25b" href="/Taanit.25b">Babli (25b</a>) and the Yerushalmi source <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 13:13" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.13.13"><i>Gen. rabba</i>13(13</a>), Rebbi Meïr is reported to require that the rains penetrate the depth of the penetration of the ploughs̮hare (which is assumed to be three hand-breadths,) whereas R. Yehudah says that for dry earth, one handb̮readth was enough, for average ground two handb̮readths, and for well worked ground, three handb̮readths. If these are two equivalent formulations, then the text here has to be interpreted in the light of the text in Babli/Midrash: Not that R. Meïr requires a rainfall of three handb̮readths (10.8 inches) in one storm (or R. Yehudah rainfalls of 3.6, 7.2, 10.8 inches, respectively) but that the moisture has to penetrate the earth to the depth indicated. In that case, “dry earth” is simply the earth after the rainless summer months, average earth is the earth after the first winter storms, and well worked ground is the earth after an uninterrupted rain of seven days (the first, second, and third fertilizing periods in the language of Tosephta Taaniot 1:4). R. Simeon ben Eleazar seems to object to both R. Meïr and R. Yehudah because rain will make the ground water level rise by twice the amount of rain water; hence, moisture in the ground can come from below as well as from above. One must assume that in the Yerushalmi also the measurements are those of moist spots in the earth and not of rainfall in the modern sense.</i>? If it fills a vessel of three handb̮readths, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Yehudah says, the first rainfall one handb̮readth, the second one two hand breadths, and the third one three handb̮readths. It has been stated<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Taanit (Lieberman) 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Taanit_(Lieberman).1.4">Tosephta 1:4</a>.</i>: Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says, there is no handb̮readths that falls from above that the earth does not raise two handb̮readths towards it. What is the reason? <i>The deep calls to the deep by the sound of Your water spouts</i><sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 42:8" href="/Psalms.42.8"><i>Ps.</i>42:8</a>. Rashi explains in Babli Taanit that clouds also are called “deep”. The Targum to <i>Ps</i>. reads: The upper abyss calls to the lower abyss by the sound of the sprinkling of your channels (Rashi <i>Ps</i>. in Romance: <i>tes canales.</i>)</i>.
Rebbi Levi said: The upper waters are male and the lower ones female. What is the reason? <i>The earth shall open, they shall bear fruit of help</i><sup class="footnote-marker">145a</sup><i class="footnote">The verse starts: <i>"The heavens should pour down from high, and the skies should flow with justice,"</i> speaking of rain. The Babli has two contradictory sermons in this matter. Rav Jehudah is reported in 6b that rain is the earth's husband because in <i>Is.</i> 55:10 it is said that rain makes the earth give birth, while Rebbi Abbahu (there and <i>Berakhot</i> 59b) says that a benediction is recited only if “the bridegroom goes towards the bride,” meaning that drops jump up from the earth towards the descending rain. [This is the traditional interpretation, given by Ashkenazic Rabbenu Gershom and North- African Rabbenu Hanan`el. Rashi, sensing the apparent contradiction to R. Levi’s statement, has a prosaic interpretation, that on both sides of the street the gutters will spout water one towards the other.]</i>. <i>The earth shall open</i>, like a female who opens before a male; <i>they should bear fruit of help</i>, that is being fruitful and multiplying; <i>and justice shall sprout,</i> that is rainfall; <i>I, the Eternal, did create it,</i> for this purpose I created it, for the wellb̮eing of the world. Rebbi Aḥa stated it in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Gamliel<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">This is the end of Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar’s text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Taanit (Lieberman) 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Taanit_(Lieberman).1.4">Tosephta 1:4</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 6b" href="/Taanit.6b">Babli 6b</a>, this passage appears as a statement of the Amora Rebbi Abbahu. The statement also is quoted in <i>Ševi`it</i>9:7 (Note 101), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 8:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.8.5.3"><i>Nedarim</i>8:6 (Note 73</a>).</i>: Why is it called “fertilizing”? Because it impregnates the earth.
<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also is copied in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 3:5:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.3.5.6"><i>Avodah zarah</i>3:6, Notes 152–153</a>. Like the entire series here it seems that the origin is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 13" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.13"><i>Gen</i>. <i>rabba</i>13</a>.</i> Rebbi Ḥanina bar Yaqqa in the name of Rav Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">In all readable sources of <i>Gen. rabba</i>“Rebbi Jehudah”. In the Genizah fragment published by M. Sokoloff (Jerusalem 1982), “… bar `Iqa, R. Berekhia in the name of Rebbi Jehudah.”</i>: The roots of wheat split the earth to a depth of 50 cubits. The soft roots of a fig tree split rock. If this is so, what do the roots of the carob or the roots of the sycamore do<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">Which grow on hard soil unfit for agriculture.</i>? Rebbi Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">In the <i>Gen. rabba</i> fragment: R. Levi.</i> said, once every thirty days the abyss wells up and drenches them. What is the reason? <i>I, the Eternal, watch over it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">The desirable vineyard.</i> <i>and water it in</i><sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 27:3" href="/Isaiah.27.3"><i>Is</i>. 27:3</a>.</i>.” [It has been stated: Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says, the earth drinks only according to its hardness.<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition from the parallel sources. In the ms. sources of <i>Gen</i>. <i>rabba</i> the name is as given here; in the Midrash prints R. Eleazar ben R. Simeon; in <i>Berakhot</i> R. Ismael ben Eleazar.</i>]
<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Taanit (Lieberman) 1:2" href="/Tosefta_Taanit_(Lieberman).1.2">Tosephta 1:2</a>. There the name tradition is switched. From the Yerushalmi text, practice follows Rebbi.</i>“If the year was deficient, one makes up for the deficiency<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">If the lunar year makes the fall festivals early in the solar year, one waits with praying for rain until two weeks after the fall equinox, in order not to pray for unnatural acts of nature. Following Rebbi one starts early in Marḥeshwan, irrespective of the solar date.</i>; otherwise one follows its order, the words of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel. Rebbi says, one always follows its order. If the year was intercalary, one follows its intercalation.” The argument of Rebbi seems inverted. There he says, one follows its order. And here he says, after its intercalation. Rebbi Ze`ira, Rebbi Yasa, Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac brings it in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, this was needed for the end<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">The month after the intercalary, the second Adar, as well as the first two weeks of Nisan, always are a time to pray for rain. One stops only on the 15<sup>th</sup> of Nisan, irrespective of the solar date.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> When the Seventeenth of Marḥeshwan arrived without rainfall, particular people<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">As described in the Halakhah.</i> started to fast. They eat and drink while it is dark and are permitted work, washing, and anointing, and wearing sandals<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">During daylight time when they were fasting. “Sandals” stand here for any leather shoes. Any of these fast-days are either Monday or Thursday. The Babli version is “started to fast three fast-days.” The version in the <i>editio princeps</i> of the Yerushalmi and reproduced here, identical to Maimonides’s autograph Mishnah, seems to imply that they were fasting every Monday and Thursday between Marḥeshwan 17 and Kislew 1.</i>, and sexual intercourse.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> These are the particular people, those who were appointed overseers<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">The classical Arabic vocalization is פִרנָאס “village head”. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 10a" href="/Taanit.10a">Babli 10a</a>/b admits only rabbis worthy to be appointed to public office.</i> over the public. Because he was appointed overseer over the public he will be heard when he prays? But since he was appointed overseer over the public and found to be honest, he is worthy to pray and be heard.
A person was tithing following the rules<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Which not may people did.</i>. Rebbi Mana said to him, stand up and say, <i>I removed the sancta from the house</i><sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:13" href="/Deuteronomy.26.13"><i>Deut</i>. 26:13</a>. One has to explain that this happened during a prayer for rain, and R. Mana wanted him to recite the entire passage, ending with <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:15" href="/Deuteronomy.26.15">v. 15</a>: <i>Look down from Your holy Place, from Heaven, and bless Your people Israel, and the Land which You gave us as You had sworn to our forefathers, a land flowing with milk and honey.</i></i>.
A person came to a relative of Rebbi Yannai and said to him: Rabbi, acquire merit with me<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">By giving me alms.</i>. He said to him, did your father not have money<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">And you are barred from taking alms.</i>? He answered, no. He told him, it is deposited with me. He told him, I heard about it that it was [repayment<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">It is not known what the original text of the scribe was. The <i>editio princeps</i> has סרקון, Arabic סרק “to steal”. The beggar did not want to take the money, either following the <i>editio princeps</i> because his father stole it, or following the corrector’s text in the ms. because it was not the father’s money since it came to the hand of the holder as repayment of a loan. The second version is preferable. (Cf. <i>Peah</i>8:9, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 5:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.5.4.4"><i>Šeqalim</i>5:6 Note 99</a>.)</i>]. He said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">Depending on the interpretation of the previous sentence, the pious party should be the leader in prayers for rain.</i>, you are worthy to pray and be heard.
It was seen by the rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">In a dream.</i>, a certain donkey driver should pray<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Be the reader in the services, described in Chapter 2, of a public fast for rain as described in Mishnah 6. This service contains parts reserved for the reader alone.</i> and rain will come. The rabbis sent and brought him. They asked him, what is your profession? He said, I am a donkey driver. They asked him, what good deed did you do? He told them, once I rented out my donkey to a certain woman, and she was crying on the road. I asked her, what is your problem? She said to me, this woman’s husband is jailed and I have to see what to do to free him<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">To bribe the jailer.</i>. I sold my donkey, gave her its value<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">Greek τιμή.</i> and told her, this is yours; free your husband and do not sin. They told him, you are worthy to pray and be heard.
It was seen by Rebbi Abbahu, Pantokaka<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">Greek παντόκακος, “all bad”.</i> shall pray and rain will come. Rebbi Abbahu sent and brought him. He asked him, what is your profession? He told him, five sins this man commits every day. He rents out prostitutes<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">Usually gentile slaves.</i>, maintains the theater<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">Greek θέατρον.</i>, brings their garments to the baths, claps his hands and dances before them, and plays cymbals<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">Greek βαβούλια (Liebermann.) The Hebrew word is misspelled in the <i>editio princeps</i> and all printed editions depending on it.</i> before them<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">Either the customers of his bordello or the public in the theater.</i>. They asked him, what good deed did you do? He told them, once I was maintaining the theater, when a woman came and stood behind a pillar, crying. I asked her, what is your problem? She said to me, this woman’s husband is jailed and I have to see what to do to free him. I sold my bed, and my bed-spread, gave her its value, and told her, this is yours; free your husband and do not sin. They told him, you are worthy to pray and be heard.
<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">Like in the preceding paragraphs the pure Aramaic of the text shows that this is suggested text of sermons. A very similar story is told in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 23" href="/Taanit.23">Babli 23a/b</a> of Abba Ḥilkiah the son of Onias the circle-drawer, a mythical holy man from Hasmonean times.</i> It was seen by the rabbis that the pious man of Kefar-Immi shall pray and rain will come. The rabbis went up to him. His house-companion<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">His wife.</i> told them, he is working on the mountain. They went out to him. They said to him “much success” but he did not answer. He sat down to eat and did not invite them to partake. When he finished he made one load of firewood and put the coat on top of the load. He went and said to his house-companion, these rabbis here want us to pray that rain should fall. But if I shall be praying and rain would fall, it would be a shame for them, and if not it would be a desecration of the Name of Heaven. Therefore come, I and you shall go up and pray. If it will rain, we shall say to them, Heaven already did wonders; and if not, we shall say to them, we are not worthy to pray and be heard. They went up, prayed, and it rained. He descended to them and asked them, why did the rabbis trouble themselves here today? They said to him, we ask that you should pray that it rain. He answered them, do you need my prayer? Heaven already did wonders. They asked him, when you were on the mountain, we said to you “much success” but you did not answer us? He told them, I was occupied in my work and did not want to divert my thoughts from my work. They asked him, and when you sat down to eat, why did you not invite us to partake? He answered them, because I had with me only my portion. What could I say to you in flattery? They asked him, why when you finished you put the coat on top of the load? He told them, because it is not mine. It is borrowed so I could pray wearing it. Why should I denigrate it? They asked him, why while you were on the mountain your wife wore dirty clothing, but when you came from the mountain she wore clean clothing<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">They took her dirty clothing as a sign that she was impure having her period and were taken aback noticing suddenly that she was pure without any purification.</i>? He told them, while I was on the mountain your wife wore dirty clothing so no man should turn his eyes to her; but when I came from the mountain she wore clean clothing so I should not look at another’s wife. They told him, it is correct that you should be heard when praying.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of Mishnaiot 5 and 6, the daytime fasts. Parallel texts are <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Taanit (Lieberman) 1:6" href="/Tosefta_Taanit_(Lieberman).1.6">Tosephta 1:6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 12" href="/Taanit.12">Babli 12a/b</a>. In the Babli sources the author is not Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel but R. Eleazar ben R. Simeon and no stipulation which allows eating after a night’s sleep is accepted.</i>: “One is permitted to eat until the East is illuminated, the words of Rebbi. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, until the cock calls<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">Approximately astronomical dawn.</i>.” What means “until the cock calls”? If he slept and awoke he is forbidden. That is, if he did not stipulate, but if he stipulated it is permitted.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the New Moon of Kislev came without rainfall the Court decrees three public fast-days. They eat and drink while it is dark and are permitted work, washing, and anointing, and wearing sandals<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">During daylight time when they were fasting. “Sandals” stand here for any leather shoes. Any of these fast-days are either Monday or Thursday. The Babli version is “started to fast three fast-days.” The version in the <i>editio princeps</i> of the Yerushalmi and reproduced here, identical to Maimonides’s autograph Mishnah, seems to imply that they were fasting every Monday and Thursday between Marḥeshwan 17 and Kislew 1.</i>, and sexual intercourse.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Joḥanan said, pregnant and nursing women do not fast on any fast-day for which the Court decides to interrupt<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">“Interrupt” means that one stops eating before sundown on the day preceding the fast; this refers to the three fast-days (Monday, Thursday, and Monday) mentioned in Mishnah 7 and the seven mentioned in Mishnah 8.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said, this seems reasonable if they originally decided not to interrupt<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">The fast-days mentioned in Mishnaiot 5, where it is understood that women do not fast, and Mishnah 6, where women are not mentioned. The fasting on these days essentially means missed lunch.</i>, but if they directly decided to interrupt, pregnant and nursing women have to fast. It was stated thus: “Pregnant and nursing women fast regularly on the Ninth of Av [and the Day of Atonement]<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition; out of place since the <i>baraita</i> concerns rabbinic fast-days only.</i> and on the first and the second groups<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">Mentioned in Mishnaiot 6 and 7.</i> of fast-days. But on the seven last ones they do not fast; nevertheless they should not spoil themselves but eat and drink for the necessities of the child.” Does this not refer to the case that they decided originally not to interrupt<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>baraita</i> explicitly states that women have to fast on fast-days which as a matter of course start in the evening. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 14a" href="/Taanit.14a">Babli 14a</a>.</i>? Rebbi Abba bar Zavda, Rebbi Isaac bar Tevlai came in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Even if they directly decided to interrupt, pregnant and nursing women do not fast<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">Denied in the Babli.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If these passed without rainfall the Court decrees three supplementary public fast-days. They eat and drink while it is daylight and are forbidden work, washing, anointing, wearing sandals, and sexual intercourse<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">Following the rules of the Day of Atonement.</i>. In addition, one closes the bath houses<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">The thermal baths.</i>.
