<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rav Jeremiah: He who does work on a public fast-day is like one who does work on the Day of Atonement. What is the reason? <i>Sanctify a fast-day, call an assembly</i><sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joel 2:15" href="/Joel.2.15"><i>Joel</i>2:15</a>.</i>. But was it not stated, the night is permitted but the day forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 14a" href="/Taanit.14a">Babli 14a</a>.</i>? Rebbi Ze`ira said, Abba bar Jeremiah<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 13b" href="/Taanit.13b">Babli 13b</a>: Rav Jeremiah bar Abba.</i> confirmed it; <i>assemble the people</i><sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joel 2:16" href="/Joel.2.16"><i>Joel</i>2:16</a>.</i>, at the time of the popular assembly.
<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 4:1:7-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.4.1.7-8"><i>Pesaḥim</i>4:1, Notes 28–37</a>.</i> If women use not to work after the end of the Sabbath, it is no {legitimate} usage; until the end of the <i>seder</i><sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">The additional prayer at the end of the evening service at the end of the Sabbath.</i> it is {legitimate} usage. On Monday and Thursday<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">Which were common fast-days of the pious in Palestine (cf. L. Ginzberg, <i>Genizah Studies in Memory of Doctor Solomon Schechter</i>, vol. 1, p. 483, §6.)</i>, it is no {legitimate} usage, to the end of the fast-day prayers it is [{legitimate} usage]. On the day of the willow twigs<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">The Seventh Day of Tabernacles. Since the following day is a holiday, it is appropriate that the preparations be finished by the time of the afternoon prayers.</i> it is not {legitimate} usage, after afternoon prayers it is [{legitimate} usage]. On the day of the New Moon it is {legitimate} usage. Rebbi Ze`ira said, if women use not to weave<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">Between the first and the tenth of Av.</i> from the start of Av it is {legitimate} usage, for the <i>šetiah</i> stone stopped to exist<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">The stone in the Holiest of Holies in the Temple.</i>. What is the reason? <i>For the woofs will be torn down</i><sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 11:3" href="/Psalms.11.3"><i>Ps</i>. 11:3</a>. If read as <i>the foundations will be torn down</i> it is appropriate for the anniversary of the destruction of the Temple.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥinena said, everything they made dependent on usage. There were acacia trees in Migdal Ṣevaya<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">Since by a Galilean tradition the Tabernacle was built in the desert from perfect logs of acacia wood (<i>Mimosa nilotica L.</i>) cut for this purpose by Jacob and his sons when they travelled to Egypt [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 94:4" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.94.4"><i>Gen</i>. <i>rabba</i>94(4</a>).]</i>. They came and asked Rebbi Ḥanania, the colleague of the rabbis, may one use them for work? He told them, since your ancestors used to treat them as forbidden, do not change the usage of your deceased ancestors. Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Abun<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">Even though all sources have R. Eleazar in the name of …, it must be … in the name of R. Eleazar.</i>. In any case which is permitted but in error he treats it as forbidden, if he asks they will permit him. But in any case where he knows that it is permitted but he has the usage to treat it as forbidden, if he asks they will not permit him<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">Since he intentionally accepted an unnecessary stringency, it has the status of a vow.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">The fast instituted to mourn the destruction of the Temple.</i>“Washing”. Ze`urah bar Ḥama, Yose the son of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: On a public fast-day<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">Since he intentionally accepted an unnecessary stringency, it has the status of a vow.</i> one washes as usual his face, hands, and feet. On the Ninth of Av<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">The fast instituted to mourn the destruction of the Temple.</i> one washes his hands and moves them over his face. On the day of Atonement one washes his hands, dries them with a towel, and moves the towel over his face. Rebbi Jonah moistened a rag and put it under the water pitcher<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">He soaked the rag before the start of the Day of Atonement, squeezed it and let it dry somewhat during the night so that the next morning it should be somewhat moist but not dripping.</i>. But did we not state that there is no difference between a public fast and the Ninth of Av except the prohibition of work at places where they were used to it<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">There were places where the community had adopted the rule that one did not work on the Ninth of Av. Nowhere was this extended to all fast days in years of drought.</i>? If somebody was going to his teacher, or to his daughter, and crossed a lake or a river he does not worry<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">On a day where washing was rabbinically forbidden. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 77b" href="/Yoma.77b"><i>Yoma</i> Babli 77b</a>, Tosephta 4:5.</i>. If his feet were dirtied he immerses them in water and does not worry. Rebbi Abba instructed following this Tanna. Rebbi Aḥa instructed that one who comes from the road and his feet are dulled, he may wash them in water. It was stated: A mourner and one in the ban<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">Any weekday of the year the mourner and the person in the ban are forbidden to wear leather shoes. The person in the ban has to follow these rules if he ever wants to have the ban lifted. Babli <i>Mo`ed Qaṭan</i>15b.</i> on a trip are permitted leather shoes. When they come to the town they shall take them off. The same applies to a public fast and the Ninth of Av<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">For everybody.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">This text is not only copied in <i>Yoma</i>, it is shortened from the main source <i>Ma`aser Šeni</i>2:1 (Notes 28–35), and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 9:4:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.9.4.2-3"><i>Šabbat</i>9, Notes 122–127</a>.</i>“Anointing.” As it was stated: On the Sabbath both anointing for pleasure and anointing not for pleasure are permitted. On the Day of Atonement, both anointing for pleasure and anointing not for pleasure are forbidden. On the Ninth of Av and public fasts, anointing for pleasure is forbidden but anointing not for pleasure is permitted. But it was stated: Anointing is equal to drinking regarding prohibition and reparation but not punishment<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">Referring to illegal use of heave and dedicated food by non-Cohanim and its replacement by 5/4 of the value taken.</i>. On the Day of Atonement regarding prohibition but not punishment<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">The only biblical prohibitions on the Day of Atonement are eating, drinking, and working. The other two, anointing and sexual relations, are rabbinic and not subject to biblical punishment.</i> But was it not stated, <i>they shall not desecrate</i><sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:15" href="/Leviticus.22.15"><i>Lev</i>. 22:15</a>. The verse refers to the non-Cohen who “eats” holy food in error. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 32a" href="/Niddah.32a">Babli <i>Niddah</i>32a</a>.</i>, to include him who anoints or drinks? Rebbi Joḥanan said, there is no “anoints” there. Rebbi Abba Mari said, if there is no “anoints” there is no “drinks”. For if it were not so, do matters combine which come from two different prohibitions<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">If the verse in <i>Lev.</i> is needed to subsume drinking under eating, it is incomprehensible that for inadvertently eating and drinking together on the Day of Atonement one should be responsible only for one sacrifice since in that case, one infringes on two separate biblical prohibitions and should be liable for two separate sacrifices. Similarly, if one illegitimately ate and drank heave he should be liable for two separate fifths. Since in both cases the Mishnah treats eating and drinking together, the verse cannot express a separate status for drinking; the addition of anointing and drinking is rabbinic interpretation but not biblical law and there is no reason to exclude anointing.</i>?
From where that there is a clear commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">If the verse in <i>Lev.</i> is needed to subsume drinking under eating, it is incomprehensible that for inadvertently eating and drinking together on the Day of Atonement one should be responsible only for one sacrifice since in that case, one infringes on two separate biblical prohibitions and should be liable for two separate sacrifices. Similarly, if one illegitimately ate and drank heave he should be liable for two separate fifths. Since in both cases the Mishnah treats eating and drinking together, the verse cannot express a separate status for drinking; the addition of anointing and drinking is rabbinic interpretation but not biblical law and there is no reason to exclude anointing.</i>? Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Simai: <i>Nor did I give from it to the dead</i><sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:14" href="/Deuteronomy.26.14"><i>Deut</i>. 26:14</a>.</i>. Where do we hold? If not to bring a casket or shrouds for him, would this be permitted for the living? Since this is forbidden for the living, therefore certainly for the dead. What is permitted to the living but forbidden to the dead? This implies that this is anointing<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 302. Second Tithe must be consumed; no other use is authorized..</i>.
“Wearing shoes”. It was stated: In all cases where they said that one may not wear shoes, when he departs on a trip he puts them on, when he arrives at a walled city he takes them off; this includes the mourner and the person in the ban<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">Babli <i>Mo`ed qaṭan</i>15b. The Yerushalmi version is quoted by <i>Or zarua</i>§277.</i>. There are Tannaim who state, one goes in slippers<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>impilia, -ium</i>(pl/) “felt slippers”.</i> on the day of Atonement; and there are Tannaim who state, one does not. Rav Ḥisda said, he who said one goes, in textile slippers, and he who said one does not go, in leather slippers<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 12:1:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.12.1.15"><i>Yebamot</i>12:1, Note 35</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 102b" href="/Yevamot.102b">Babli <i>Yebamot</i>102b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman visited Rebbi Joshua ben Levi in the night of the Great Fast<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote">The day of Atonement.</i>; he came to him wearing laced shoes<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">A leather sole with a textile upper part held together by laces.</i>. He asked him, what is this? He answered, I am asthenic<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ʼασθενής. Since the rules of not wearing leather shoes are rabbinic, they are waved for health reasons.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman visited Rebbi Joshua ben Levi in the night of a fast day<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">A rabbinic fast day.</i>; he came to him wearing laced shoes. He asked him, what is this? He answered, I am asthenic. They saw Rebbi Shammai walking in the night of a fast day in laced shoes. A student of Rebbi Mana instructed a relative of the Patriarch to wear laced shoes<sup class="footnote-marker">221a</sup><i class="footnote">Without a medical condition which would justify waving the rules.</i>. He asked him, from where? From Rebbi Joshua ben Levi. For Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, I am asthenic.
<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">From here on there exists a much shortened version in an Ashkenazi ms. (A) published by J. Sussman, <i>Kobez al Yad</i>12(22), 1994, pp. 43ff.</i>“And sexual relations.” Come and see, he is forbidden to wash, not so much more to have sexual relations<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Day of Atonement is a day of prayer and by an institution of Ezra prayer was forbidden after sexual relations before immersion in a <i>miqweh</i>, the prohibition of washing should imply the prohibition of sexual relations without the need to spell it out.</i>? Explain it that it was a place where one does not immerse oneself, or before Ezra instituted immersion for people having had an emission. <sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">This text to the end of the paragraph is also from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.4.5"><i>Berakhot</i>3:4 (Notes 167–168</a>) which is the original source.</i> Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi.: <i>Qeri</i> is only from sexual intercourse. Rav Huna said, even if he saw himself enjoying in his dream. They wanted to say, only from a woman. Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose both say, even from something else. There, we did state: “On the Day of Atonement eating, drinking, washing, anointing, wearing shoes, and sexual relations, are forbidden”? And it was stated in that respect: Men with <i>qeri</i> immerse themselves secretly in their normal way on the day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Yoma (Lieberman) 4:5" href="/Tosefta_Yoma_(Lieberman).4.5">Tosephta <i>Kippurim</i>4:5</a> and Babli 88a the reading is: “Men with <i>qeri</i>(emission of semen) immerse themselves normally on Yom Kippur”; one speaks of a full immersion and “in secret” is not mentioned. The Tosephta is Babylonian formulation.</i>. Does this not contradict Rebbi Joshua ben Levi? Explain it if he had intercourse on the previous day and forgot and did not immerse himself. But it was stated: It happened that one saw Rebbi Yose bar Ḥalaphta immersing himself secretly on Yom Kippur. Can you say about that holy body that he forgot<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, the interpretation of Rav Huna is incorrect and that of rabbis Yose (the Amora) and Jonah is correct.<br>Here ends the <i>Yoma</i> text.</i>?
<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gen. rabba</i>31(17), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 6:9-11:32" href="/Genesis.6.9-11.32"><i>Tanḥuma Noaḥ</i>11</a>, with different name traditions. Also in A the attributions are different.</i> Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi, Rebbi Ḥanin in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: On his entrance to the Ark Noe was forbidden sexual relations. What is the reason? <i>You shall come into the Ark, you, your sons, your wife, and your sons’ wives</i><sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 6:18" href="/Genesis.6.18"><i>Gen</i>. 6:18</a>.</i>. On his exit sexual relations were permitted to him. What is the reason? <i>Leave the Ark, you, your wife, your sons, and your sons’ wives with you</i><sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 8:16" href="/Genesis.8.16"><i>Gen</i>. 8:16</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, <i>by their families they left the Ark</i><sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 8:19" href="/Genesis.8.19"><i>Gen</i>. 8:19</a>.</i>; since they refrained from their relations they merited to be saved from the Deluge. You may know that it is so since we stated<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 108b" href="/Sanhedrin.108b">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i>108b</a>.</i>, Ham, the dog, and the raven misbehaved. Ham exited charcoal colored. The dog exited public in his relations. The raven exited different from the creatures. Rebbi Abun<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Gen. rabba</i> Abbin.</i> said, it is written, <i>in want and hunger infertile</i><sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 30:3" href="/Job.30.3"><i>Job</i>30:3</a>.</i>. If you see that want comes into the world make your wife infertile<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">Formulated as a general prohibition <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 11a" href="/Taanit.11a">Babli 11a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 108b" href="/Sanhedrin.108b"><i>Sanhedrin</i>108b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Levi<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 11a" href="/Taanit.11a">Babli 11a</a> in the name of R. Simeon ben Laqish. Those who “desire children” are whose who yet have none.</i> said, <i>to Joseph were born two sons</i><sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 41:50" href="/Genesis.41.50"><i>Gen</i>. 41:50</a>.</i>, when? <i>Before the start of the year of famine</i>. It was stated in the name of Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 11a" href="/Taanit.11a">Babli 11a</a> in the name of R. Simeon ben Laqish. Those who “desire children” are whose who yet have none.</i>: those who desire to have children do have sexual relations. Rebbi Yose said, only on the day she immersed herself<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote">A day of maximal probability of conception; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 27a" href="/Sotah.27a">Babli <i>Sotah</i>27a</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If these passed without rainfall the Court decrees seven supplementary public fast-days<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote">On successive Mondays and Thursdays.</i> for a total of thirteen. Additional to the prior ones on these one blows horns and locks the stores. On Mondays one relaxes at nightfall<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote">The grocer may open the store to sell food but not display his wares.</i>; on Thursdays they are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote">The seller of edibles may sell during the entire Thursday to provide food for the Sabbath.</i> because of the honor of the Sabbath.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Yudan son of Rebbi Ḥama of Kefar Teḥamin, like the forty which Moses spent on the Mountain<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">On Sabbath one may not fast. This means that Monday and Thursday together represent a week of 6 days; every actual fast day represents three days, and 3x13=39, “like forty”, approximately 40.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, because one does not excessively impose on the community<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote">For him the number 13 is for a practical reason, not the symbolic one given by the aggadic preacher. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 14b" href="/Taanit.14b">Babli 14b</a> as tannaitic statement, not unopposed.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, for if there were two causes, for example a lack of rain and locusts, one blows for both<sup class="footnote-marker">243</sup><i class="footnote">If a swarm of locusts should appear in a dry winter, one combines prayers relative to both calamities and never exceeds the number of 13 public fasts even for multiple causes.<br>If this is an explanation of the previous statement, one has to read “Yasa”, one of R. Ḥiyya’s teachers, instead of “Yose”, R. Ḥiyya’s student’s student’s student.</i>. And why for these two occasions<sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote">These are mentioned explicitly to be combined.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, for these two cause prices to increase.
“On Mondays one relaxes at nightfall,” he opens one wing and locks the other<sup class="footnote-marker">245</sup><i class="footnote">The grocer opens his store but does not turn the shutter into a selling platform.</i>. “On Thursdays they are permitted.” How? He opens one wing and locks the other, or he opens both<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote">The store is open the entire day but whether one turns the shutter into a selling platform and displays one’s wares is not decided and therefore depends on local custom.</i>?

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If these passed without rainfall one decreases commercial activity, building, planting, preliminary and definitive marriage, and greetings between people, as persons reprimanded by the Omnipresent. Particular people<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">As described in the Halakhah.</i> continue to fast up to the end of Nisan. If it rained after the end of Nisan it is a sign<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Greek σημεῑον.</i> of curse as it is said, <i>is today not harvest of wheat</i><sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 12:17" href="/I_Samuel.12.17"><i>1S</i>. 12:17</a>, where Samuel invokes summer rain as a sign of Divine displeasure about the people’s demand for a king.</i>?
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, that is a building of enjoyment. But if his wall was inclined, he tears it down and rebuilds it<sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 12:17" href="/I_Samuel.12.17"><i>1S</i>. 12:17</a>, where Samuel invokes summer rain as a sign of Divine displeasure about the people’s demand for a king.</i>. Samuel said, the wall were he sleeps<sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote">He requires construction only if there is danger to life.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">The parallels are in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 1:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.1.1.7"><i>Ketubot</i>1:1 (Notes 34–42</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 5:2:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.5.2.12"><i>Yom Tov</i>5:2 (Notes 65ff</a>.)</i> Rebbi Abba bar Cohen said before Rebbi Yose: Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Jacob bar Idi: A man may not preliminarily marry on a Friday. That means, to make an engagement feast<sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote">Since this would impinge on the Sabbath meal. It is in order to make the preliminary marriage on Friday and arrange the festive meal as Sabbath meal.</i>. This implies that the preliminary marriage itself is permitted. Samuel says, even on the Ninth of Ab<sup class="footnote-marker">251</sup><i class="footnote">The anniversary of the destruction of both Temples.</i> a preliminary marriage is permitted, lest another forestall him. The argument of Samuel seems inverted. There<sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote">A similar argument is in the Babli, <i>Mo`ed qaṭan</i>18b.</i>, he says, <i>God puts singles in a house</i>; <i>To rise on scales; they all are of vapor!</i><sup class="footnote-marker">253</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 68:6" href="/Psalms.68.6"><i>Ps</i>. 68:6</a>; 62:10. This means that marriages are pre-ordained in Heaven. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 29:5" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.29.5"><i>Lev. rabba</i>29(5</a>).)</i> And here, he says so<sup class="footnote-marker">254</sup><i class="footnote">If marriages are pre-ordained, why should anybody be afraid that another man could snatch the bride pre-selected for him? Another man might by his prayer cause the Heavenly decree to be changed, but this would lead to the early death of one of the partners of the marriage. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 2a" href="/Sotah.2a">Babli <i>Soṭa</i>2a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 22a" href="/Sanhedrin.22a"><i>Sanhedrin</i>22a</a>.</i>? That means, that he should not forestall him in prayer. Even so, it would not be permanent.
(Greetings) [Halakhah 9:]<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, to be deleted.</i> Was it not stated<sup class="footnote-marker">256</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Taanit (Lieberman) 3:12" href="/Tosefta_Taanit_(Lieberman).3.12">Tosephta 3:12</a>.<br>The implication is that on a full fast day, from evening to evening, greeting is frowned upon. Then it should have been unnecessary to mention this in the Mishnah.</i>: “One does not greet fellows on the Ninth of Av, but one answers common people in a soft manner“<sup class="footnote-marker">257</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ʼιδιώτης “individual”, a term for the unlearned. One greats them in a weak voice, not to be impolite but hinting that greeting is undesirable on this day. “Fellow” is the appellation of a person careful (and therefore knowledgeable) in matters of tithes and impurity, cf. Introduction to Tractate Demay.</i>? It is needed for the days in between<sup class="footnote-marker">258</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah teaches to refrain from personal greetings all the days of a drought, not only on the Mondays and Thursdays of actual fasts.</i>.
“After the end of Nisan it is a sign of curse.” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, only after a Nisan of equinox<sup class="footnote-marker">259</sup><i class="footnote">If the spring equinox was in Nisan. But if the month named “Nisan” really should have been the second Adar, one stops praying for rain only after the spring equinox, or if the equinox was in Adar, one has to stop fasting at the end of this month.<br>Since the calendar rules were promulgated by R. Yose, preceding R. Yose ben R. Abun, one may read the statement as a criticism of these rules which occasionally permit such a deviation. (But it must be remembered that the detailed rules which have come down to us were written up by authors who lived at least 600 years after R. Yose.)</i>. Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac said, this you are saying if it did not rain earlier<sup class="footnote-marker">260</sup><i class="footnote">When rain was really needed.</i>. But if it rained earlier it is a sign of blessing.

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How is the order of the fast days? One removes the Ark<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This statement confirms that in Mishnaic times the Ark containing the Torah scrolls was a movable chest, not part of the synagogue building. This is confirmed by the archeological evidence that in early Galilean synagogues the entrance was in the South. For prayers the Ark was moved from the storage room to the entrance; the reader stood before the Ark, and everybody was praying in the direction of Jerusalem, facing the Ark. In Amoraic times the Ark became part of the building in the South wall and the entrance was moved to the North.</i> to the town plaza<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The town square accommodating a maximum number of people.</i> and gives burnt ashes<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah explicitly excludes dust which is not ash.</i> on top of the Ark, and on the head of the Patriarch, and on the the head of the presiding judge; also each individual takes and puts on his head. The Elder among them gives an exhortatory sermon: Our brothers, it is not said about the people of Nineveh, God saw their sackcloth and their fasting, but <i>God saw their deeds that they repented their evil ways</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jonah 3:10" href="/Jonah.3.10"><i>Jonah</i>3:10</a>.</i>, and what does it say in prophecy? <i>And tear your hearts and not your clothes, and return to the Eternal, your God</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joel 2:13" href="/Joel.2.13"><i>Joel</i>2:13</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “How is the order of the fast days,” etc. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, why does one move to the town plaza? To say, we are considered as if exiled before You. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, because they prayed in privacy and were not answered; therefore they went public and were publicized. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, why does one bring the Ark to the town plaza? To say that we had one precious vessel and our sins caused it to become debased. The Elder Rebbi Ḥuna of Sepphoris said, our forefathers covered it with gold and we covered it with ashes. Rebbi Jacob the Southerner said, why does one blow horns? To say, we are considered as if bellowing like animals before You. Rebbi Levi said, and why does one visit graves? To say, we are considered like dead before You. Rebbi Tanḥuma says, all of this is included. If we are guilty to die there are the dead, if exile there is exile, if hunger there is hunger<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">All these texts are sermon concepts. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 16a" href="/Taanit.16a">Babli 16a</a>.</i>.
“One gives burnt ashes<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah explicitly excludes dust which is not ash.</i> on top of the Ark.” Because of <i>with him I am in trouble</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 91:15" href="/Psalms.91.15"><i>Ps.</i>91:15</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 15b" href="/Taanit.15b">Babli 15b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira said, any time that I saw them doing this my body shuddered. In the days of Rebbi Ila they left the Ark standing and went away. Rebbi Ze`ira said to him, was it not stated: they should not take turns during the day but one person should sit there and watch it during the entire day<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">One has to date the abolition of this ceremony to his days.</i>?
Rebbi Yudan ben Rebbi Menashe and Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman. One said, to mention the merit of Abraham; the other one said, to mention the merit of Isaac<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">His willingness to be sacrificed on Mount Moriah.</i>. He who said, to mention the merit of Abraham, either ashes or dust, because of <i>I am dust and ashes</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 18:27" href="/Genesis.18.27"><i>Gen</i>. 18:27</a>. Cf. <i>Gen. rabba</i>49(23), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 16a" href="/Taanit.16a">Babli 16a</a>.</i>. He who said, to mention the merit of Isaac, only ashes. One sees the ashes of Isaac as if his ashes were collected on the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Since the only reason that he was not burned was the intervention of the angel, his merit is to have been burned.</i>. When Rebbi Judah bar Pazi went on a fast day; he said before them, our Brethren, anybody whom the beadle does not reach should take dust and put it on his head<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah requires that the synagogue personnel distribute ashes to everybody; but if the supply of ashes runs out one may substitute dust for ashes since according to one opinion the two are equivalent.</i>.
“And on the head of the Patriarch.” Rebbi Taḥlifa Caesarean said, to give it prominence. One cannot compare self-abasement with abasement by the hands of others<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah requires that the ashes be applied by others.</i>.
It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joel 2:16" href="/Joel.2.16"><i>Joel</i>2:16</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 15b-16a" href="/Taanit.15b-16a">Babli 15b/16a</a>.</i>: <i>May the bridegroom leave his room</i>, <i>and the bride her bridal chamber. May the bridegroom leave his room</i>, that is the Ark, <i>and the bride her bridal chamber</i>, that is the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In A: the Torah scroll.</i>. Another word: <i>May the bridegroom leave his room</i>, that is the Patriarch, <i>and the bride her bridal chamber</i>, that is the presiding judge. Rebbi Ḥelbo said to Rebbi Yudan the Patriarch<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">R. Judah II, Rebbi’s grandson.</i>, come with us and your pain will be gone<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">In A and the quote by R. Nission Gerondi <i>ad loc</i>.: will be effective.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, this implies that our fasts are no real fasts. Why? since the Patriarch is not with us<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Patriarch resided in Sepphoris but the Academy was in Tiberias. The implication seems to be that other details of the Mishnah also do not have to be executed exactly as described.</i>.
One verse says, <i>in front of the cover</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:2" href="/Leviticus.16.2"><i>Lev</i>. 16:2</a>. (But maybe <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:17" href="/Leviticus.4.17"><i>Lev</i>. 4:17</a> was intended.)</i>, but another verse says, <i>in front of the holy gobelin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:6" href="/Leviticus.4.6"><i>Lev</i>. 4:6</a>, the purification offering of the anointed High Priest.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa said, Rebbi Abbahu and the rabbis disagree. One said, if the Patriarch sins, majesty remains in place. If the community sinned, majesty is not in place<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 41b" href="/Zevachim.41b">Babli, <i>Zevaḥim</i>41b</a>, the idea is formulated so: If an individual revolts, the king’s government is unchanged; if an entire country revolts, the king’s government is abolished.</i>. But the other one said, because teacher and student sinned, they have to go public and be noticed, following <i>and he shall remove the bull to outside the camp</i><sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:21" href="/Leviticus.4.21"><i>Lev</i>. 4:21</a>.</i>. Ḥizqiah stated: it is a hint. Anywhere there is an anointed one there is the Ark<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">As long as anointed High Priests officiated in the Temple, the Ark was in the Holiest of Holies. In the Second Temple, whose High Priests were invested but not anointed, there was no Ark.</i>. Anywhere there is no anointed one there is no Ark. This follows what Rebbi Samuel ben Aina said in the name of Rebbi Aḥa<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Makkot 2:6:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Makkot.2.6.12"><i>Makkot</i>2:7 (Notes 124–130</a>), <i>Horaiot</i>3:2, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 21b" href="/Yoma.21b">Babli <i>Yoma</i>21b</a></i>: Five things was the last Temple missing which were in the first Temple, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Haggai 1:8" href="/Haggai.1.8"><i>Hag.</i>1:8</a>.</i>: <i>I shall be pleased with it and I will be honored</i>. It is written <i>I will be honored</i>, without the letter <i>he</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketib</i> וְאֶכָּבֵד, <i>Qere</i> וְאֶכָּֽבְדָ֖ה. Both spellings make sense. The missing ה is interpreted in the Alexandrian system of numeration as “5”.</i>. These are the five things which the last Temple was missing which were in the first Temple. They are: The {heavenly} fire, the Ark, Urim and Tummim, anointing oil<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">It is stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:23" href="/Exodus.30.23"><i>Ex.</i>30:23</a> that only Moses himself could compound this oil and in v. 31 that it should be used for all subsequent generations. By tradition, Josia buried the oil flask together with the Ark of the Covenant in the Temple Mount (<a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 35:3" href="/II_Chronicles.35.3"><i>2Chr.</i>35:3</a>) after the prophetess Hulda informed him of the imminent destruction of the Temple.</i>, and the Holy Spirit.
Explanation<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δήλωμα. A shortened version is in <i>Thr. rabba</i>3(33).</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Zavda, Rebbi Tanḥum bar Ilai, and Rebbi Joshia came to the fast. Rebbi Abba bar Zavda preached: <i>Let us lift our hearts on the palms</i><sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Lamentations 3:41" href="/Lamentations.3.41"><i>Thr</i>. 3:41</a>.</i>. Is this possible? May a person take his heart and put it on his hand? So what means “let us lift”? Let us treat our hearts equal to our hands<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Which one always washes to keep them clean.</i>, and then <i>to God in Heaven.</i> So if somebody would hold a crawling animal<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">A dead crawling animal as enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:29-30" href="/Leviticus.11.29-30"><i>Lev</i>. 11:29–30</a>, as source of original impurity. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 16a" href="/Taanit.16a">Babli 16a</a>.</i> in his hand, even if he immerses himself in the waters of Siloam or of the ocean, he never can become pure<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">In A one reads: even if he immerses himself in all oceans he never will be pure. If he throws it from his hands, even if he only immerses himself in the waters of the Siloam which are 1x1x2 cubits, he immediately is pure.</i>. But if he throws it<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The dead crawling animal.</i> from his hand he immediately is pure. Rebbi Tanḥum bar Ilai preached: <i>The ministers of Israel and the King submitted and said, the Eternal is just. And when the Eternal saw that they submitted, the Eternal’s word was to Shemaya as oracle</i><sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 12:6-7" href="/II_Chronicles.12.6-7"><i>2Chr</i>. 12:6–7</a>.</i>. It is not written “their fast” but <i>they submitted, I shall not destroy them.</i> Rebbi Joshia preached: <i>Become improved and improve</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Zephaniah 2:1" href="/Zephaniah.2.1"><i>Zeph</i>. 2:1</a>.</i>, let us purify ourselves before we purify others<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 18a" href="/Sanhedrin.18a">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i>18a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 19a" href="/Sanhedrin.19a">19a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 60b" href="/Bava_Batra.60b"><i>Bava batra</i> 60b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 107b" href="/Bava_Metzia.107b"><i>Bava meṣi`a</i>107b</a>.</i>. Since here are people present who calumniate me before Rebbi Joḥanan. But every single one is called to justice. Attending were Rebbi Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Bar Abba.</i>, Rebbi Issy<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Usually he is called Yasa, in the Babli Assi.</i>, and Rebbi Immi. They got up and left<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">What offended them in R. Joshia’s sermon is everybody’s guess; each commentator has his own opinion.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 6:9-11:32" href="/Genesis.6.9-11.32">Tanḥuma Noaḥ 8</a>, Tanḥuma Buber Noaḥ 13, <i>Gen. rabba</i>44(15), <i>Eccl. rabba</i>7(59). In a different context, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 10:2:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.10.2.7"><i>Sanhedrin</i>10:2 (Notes 169–171</a>). Differently, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rosh Hashanah 16b" href="/Rosh_Hashanah.16b">Babli <i>Roš Haššanah</i>16b</a>. The Tanḥuma text seems to be the source for Moses ben Kalonymos’s poem וּנְתַנֶּה תּוֹקֶף.</i> Rebbi Eleazar said, three things annul the harsh decree. They are: prayer, charity and repentance. All three are from one verse<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 7:14" href="/II_Chronicles.7.14"><i>2Chr</i>. 7:14</a>.</i>: <i>My people, over whom My Name is called, will submit and pray,</i> this is prayer<i>, and desire my presence,</i> this is charity as you are saying, <i>by charity I shall see Your presence</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 17:15" href="/Psalms.17.15"><i>Ps</i>. 17:15</a>.</i>. <i>They will repent their evil ways,</i> this is repentance. When they act in this way, what is written there? <i>Then I shall hear in Heaven and forgive their sins and heal their land</i>.
Rebbi Ḥaggai always preached the following from Rebbi Eleazar. Rebbi Eleazar said, the end of the year is like the year. From the dust of summer to winter, if North wind blows<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">At a time when the West wind should bring rain.</i> take care of your children. Any year for which no <i>shofar</i> was blown at the start, one will have to blow at the end<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">If one fails to obey the religious commandment to blow the <i>shofar</i> on New Year’s Day one will have to blow later as a call of calamity and emergency. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rosh Hashanah 16b" href="/Rosh_Hashanah.16b">Babli <i>Roš Haššanah</i>16b</a>.</i>. Any which does not care for the produce of the preceding will at the end lose its produce<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">If heave and tithes are not given according to the rules after the harvest of the preceding year, the yield of the following year is in jeopardy.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa said, and most of its wine will turn to vinegar. There were old men in Sepphoris who at the first rainy period<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The text A reads: רִבִיעֲתָא קַדְמִייָתָא נַחְתָּה “when the first rains came down”. The character of A makes it questionable whether this is text or a gloss.</i> were smelling the dust and could tell the {amount of} rains of the year.
Rebbi Eleazar said: <i>Is that a fast day which I would prefer</i>, that is <i>a day where a person deprives himself?</i> This is not a day to my liking. What is a day to my liking? <i>Is not this a day I would prefer, opening chains of evil, unbinding fetters of the yoke. Is it not breaking your bread for the hungry, and bring to the house the poor persecuted ones</i>. What is written after this? <i>Then you will call and the Eternal will answer</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 58:5" href="/Isaiah.58.5"><i>Is</i>. 58:5</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 58:7" href="/Isaiah.58.7">7</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 58:9" href="/Isaiah.58.9">9</a>. The spelling פרוש against masoretic פרס is confirmed by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 9a" href="/Bava_Batra.9a">Babli <i>Bava batra</i>9a</a> and is not to be explained away (cf. <i>Mesoret Hashas Babli</i>9a, Note 2.)</i> etc.
Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 2:6" href="/Genesis.2.6"><i>Gen</i>. 2:6</a>.</i>: <i>and distress</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">He identifies אֵד “fog” and אֵיד “<i>dies ater”.</i> In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 13:15" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.13.15"><i>Gen. rabba</i>13(15</a>) the entire paragraph is attributed to R. Berekhiah.</i> <i>rose from the earth</i>. If rupture rises from the earth, rain will fall immediately. Rebbi Berekhiah said, it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 32:2" href="/Deuteronomy.32.2"><i>Deut</i>. 32:2</a>.</i>: <i>may my teaching drip like rain</i>, because if one confesses in repentance<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The homily identifies ערף “to drip” (Accadic <i>arapu</i>“to be cloudy”) with another root ערף (Arabic ערף “to know”, אעתרף “to confess”.)</i> rain will fall immediately. Rebbi Berekhiah made thirteen fast days but no rain fell; at the end locusts came. He went and said before them, our brethren, look at what we are doing. Is that not what the prophet needles us<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Micah 7:3-4" href="/Micah.7.3-4"><i>Micah</i>7:3–4</a>.</i>, <i>from evil hands to profit</i>, we damage one another with our hands and expect good things; <i>the noble asks</i> where is the bribe to be collected, <i>and the judge in payment</i>, pay me and I shall pay you, <i>the great one speaks lies from his soul and they will distort it</i>, they will turn it thick, they turned it into a plaiting of debts. And who can stop it? <i>Their best one is like thorns</i>, the best one among them is like thorns, <i>the straight one like a hedge of thorns,</i> the most upright among them is like those branches, <i>on the day to which they looked comes their due</i>, on the day which we expected relief locusts, <i>this shall be their consternation</i>! From the midst of their crying, and crying, and crying, there fell rain.
Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, the people of Nineveh repented in trickery. What did they do<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 16a" href="/Taanit.16a">Babli 16a</a>.</i>? Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Ḥalfuta<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">In A: R. Ḥanania in the name of Resh Laqish.</i>: They put calves inside and their mothers outside, foals inside and their mothers outside; these were mooing from one side and those mooing from the other side. They said, if He does not have mercy on us we shall not have mercy on them. That is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joel 1:18" href="/Joel.1.18"><i>Joel</i> 1:18</a>.</i>, <i>how the animals groan, are cattle herds confused</i>, etc. Rebbi Aḥa said, that is what they do in Arabia. <i>They should be covered by sack-cloth, humans and animals, and call on God in force</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jonah 3:8" href="/Jonah.3.8"><i>Jonah</i> 3:8</a>.</i>. What means “in force”? Rebbi Simeon ben Ḥalfuta said, the brash one wins over the proper one<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 105a" href="/Sanhedrin.105a">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 105a</a>.</i>, so much more if it is for the good of the world. <i>Each man shall repent his evil ways and robbery in their hands</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, what they had in their hands they returned, what was in a chest, a trunk and a cupboard they did not return.
It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joel 2:13" href="/Joel.2.13"><i>Joel</i> 2:13</a>.</i>, <i>and tear your hearts instead of your garments and return to the Eternal, your God, for He is compassionate and merciful</i>. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, if you tear up your hearts in repentance you will not have to tear your garments about your sons or daughters<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">They will not die young.</i>, only about the Eternal, your God. Why? <i>For He is compassionate and merciful, slow in anger, full of love, and sorrow about evil</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 22a" href="/Eruvin.22a">Babli <i>Eruvin</i>22a</a>.</i> Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: It is not written here “slow in one anger” but “slow in anger”. He is slow in anger with the just ones, and slow in anger with the evil ones. Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Tanḥum ben Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It is not written here “slow in one anger” but “slow in anger”. He is slow in anger before he starts to collect. Once He starts to collect he is slow to complete His collection. <sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 5:1:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.5.1.12"><i>Šeqalim</i>5:2 Note 39</a>, <i>Yom Tov</i>3:9 <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.2.3.2">Note 124</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 50" href="/Bava_Kamma.50">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i>50a/b</a>.</i> Rebbi Ḥanina said, anybody who says that the All-Merciful is indulgent, his intestines shall dissolve themselves; for He is forbearing and then collects His due. Rebbi Levi said, what means “forbearing”? He is far from being angry<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 5:1:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.5.1.12"><i>Šeqalim</i>5:2 Note 39</a>, <i>Yom Tov</i>3:9 <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.2.3.2">Note 124</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 50" href="/Bava_Kamma.50">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i>50a/b</a>.</i>. {A parable of} a king who had two violent legions. Said the king, if they dwell with me at the capital, if the people of the capital would anger me they would get up and decimate them. Therefore I shall send them to a faraway place. Then if the people of the capital would anger me, until I shall send for them the people of the capital will pacify me and I will accept their excuses. [So the Holy One, praise to Him, said, anger and rage are angels of destruction. Therefore I shall send them to a faraway place. Therefore if Israel would anger me, until I shall send for them and bring them, Israel will repent and I shall accept their repentance.]<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition; no source known for it.</i> That is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 13:5" href="/Isaiah.13.5"><i>Is</i>. 13:5</a>.</i>, <i>they come from a far country, from the ends of heaven</i><sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The verse continues: <i>The Eternal and the instruments of His rage, to destroy the earth</i>.</i>, etc. Rebbi Isaac said, not only that but He locked them up. That is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 50:25" href="/Jeremiah.50.25"><i>Jer</i>. 50:25</a>.</i>, <i>the Eternal opened His storehouse and removed the vessels of his wrath</i>. By the time He opened and He took pains, His mercies take over. It was stated in the name of Rebbi Meïr: <i>For the Eternal left His place</i><sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 26:21" href="/Isaiah.26.21"><i>Is</i>. 26:21</a>.</i>, He went from mode to mode. He left the mode of judgement and came to the mode of mercy<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The designation of the mode of judgment is the name <i>Elohim</i>“supreme power”, referring to His rule of the forces of nature. The designation of the mode of mercy is <i>YHWH</i>“Eternal”.</i> for Israel.
It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 23:19" href="/Numbers.23.19"><i>Num</i>. 23:19</a>. In this verse Balaam seems to proclaim an extreme doctrine of predestination which negates the possibility of effective repentance, against the homiletics described in the Halakhah. <i>Tanḥuma Mas`e</i>6.</i>, <i>God is no human that He would lie</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman and the rabbis. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman said, if the Holy One, praise to Him, says to give a benefit, <i>God is no human that He would lie</i>. If he said to make a detriment, <i>He would say but not do it, and speak but not keep it</i>. But the rabbis are saying, there is no man who could make God’s words as if nonexistent. <i>Why should Your anger burn against Your people</i><sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 32:11" href="/Exodus.32.11"><i>Ex</i>. 32:11</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 23:8" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.23.8"><i>Num. rabba</i>23(8</a>).</i>? <i>Or a human and He be sorrow</i>. Not the son of Amram who made God to have second thoughts: <i>the Eternal had second thoughts about the evil He had said to bring over His people</i><sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 32:14" href="/Exodus.32.14"><i>Ex</i>. 32:14</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu said, if a man says to you, I am a god, he lies. I am the Son of Man<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The expression, in imitation of Accadic <i>awelum</i>, “free man”, by which Ezechiel was addressed by God as true prophet.</i>, at the end he will regret it. That I ascend to heaven, “this he says but cannot fulfill.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">An imitation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 23:19" href="/Numbers.23.19"><i>Num</i>. 23:19</a>.</i>”
Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rav: Today there is no fast-day<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Patriarch resided in Sepphoris but the Academy was in Tiberias. The implication seems to be that other details of the Mishnah also do not have to be executed exactly as described.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, does this mean that the fast-days which we are arranging are not those? He said to him, so says Rav: The verse says about any fast-day which does not follow its rules, <i>she was noisy around me, therefore I hated her</i><sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">An imitation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 23:19" href="/Numbers.23.19"><i>Num</i>. 23:19</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> After they stood to pray they place before the Ark an old knowledgeable man who has children but his house is empty<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">He has to be poor but must be knowledgeable enough to be able to recite all prayers without slowing down or interruption, and must have young children who are dependent on him.</i> so that his mind should be concentrated on prayer. He recites before them 24 benedictions, eighteen of the daily prayers and additional six. These are they, <i>Zikhronot</i> and <i>Shofarot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The two additional benedictions in the <i>musaf</i> prayer of New Year’s Day; each one accompanied by the blowing of the <i>shofar</i>. The text of each addition is followed by an invocation and a doxology as spelled out in Mishnaiot 4–10.</i>. <i>To the Eternal in my straights</i><sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 120" href="/Psalms.120"><i>Ps</i>. 120</a>.</i>. <i>I lifted my eyes to the Mountains</i><sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 121" href="/Psalms.121"><i>Ps</i>. 121</a>.</i>. <i>From the depths I called on You, Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 130" href="/Psalms.130"><i>Ps</i>. 130</a>.</i><i>. Prayer of the poor who is fainting</i><sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 102" href="/Psalms.102"><i>Ps</i>. 102</a>.</i>
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: “And modest, easy on youth, used to study, used to preaching and owner of house and field<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 16a" href="/Taanit.16a">Babli 16a</a>.</i>.” We have stated, “but his house is empty”, and you are saying so<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah seems to imply that he is a renter and does not own anything.</i>? If nobody is available one may place anybody one wants<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Since usually nobody available fills all qualifications stated in Mishnah and <i>baraita</i>, the community organizer may appoint any person to be reader.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The remainder of this Halakhah is parallel to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 4:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.4.3.2">Halakhah 4:3</a> in <i>Berakhot</i>(Notes 138–181, <b>ב</b>). The text is quoted at length in <i>Tanḥuma Wayyera</i> and from there in midrashic literature. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 28b" href="/Berakhot.28b">Babli <i>Berakhot</i>28b</a></i> And why eighteen? Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, corresponding to the eighteen psalms that are written from the start of Psalms to <i>May the Eternal listen to you on the day of trouble</i><sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 20:2" href="/Psalms.20.2"><i>Ps</i>. 20:2</a>.</i>. If somebody would say to you they are nineteen, tell him <i>Why are</i>&lt;<i>the Gentiles</i>&gt;<i>in upheaval</i><sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 2:1" href="/Psalms.2.1"><i>Ps</i>. 2:1</a>.</i> is not in the count<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">In most Talmudic sources <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 1" href="/Psalms.1">Psalms 1</a> and 2 form one song. To compensate, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 116" href="/Psalms.116"><i>Ps</i>. 116</a> is split into two. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 9b" href="/Berakhot.9b">Babli <i>Berakhot</i>9b</a>.</i>. From here they said that one who prayed and was not answered needs to fast<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Since the “trouble” referred to in the Psalm is interpreted as fasting.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, a hint that a Torah student must say to his teacher: May your prayers be answered<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>Ps</i>. 20 is read as addressed to David.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts an extensive Genizah text edited by Ginzberg in his <i>Yerushalmi Fragments</i>, New York 1909 (G).</i> Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">While the name of R. Joshua ben Levi is missing in G and the parallel text <b>ב</b>, in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 28b" href="/Berakhot.28b">Babli <i>Berakhot</i>28b</a> the statement is ascribed to the school of R. Joshua ben Levi, whose great authority turns this haggadic statement into halakhic prescription.</i>: corresponding to the eighteen bones in the spine since when a person prays he has to bend all of them bowing down. What is the reason? <i>All my bones shall say, Eternal, who is like You</i><sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 35:10" href="/Psalms.35.10"><i>Ps</i>. 35:10</a>.</i><i>?</i>
Rebbi Levi<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Berakhot (Lieberman) 3:25" href="/Tosefta_Berakhot_(Lieberman).3.25">Tosephta (<i>Berakhot</i>3:25</a>) this is a tannaitic text together with the later quote about composite benedictions; in the Babli (<i>loc. cit</i>.) it is attributed to the school of R. Samuel bar Naḥman, and by implication to the latter’s teacher, the first generation Amora R. Jonathan.</i> said, corresponding to the eighteen Divine Names<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">In biblical language, אזכרה is the part of a cereal sacrifice burned on the altar (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:2" href="/Leviticus.2.2"><i>Lev</i>. 2:2</a>). It is best explained from Accadic <i>zekrun</i>“pronounced name”, since the part to be burned is taken ceremoniously in God’s Name. The same explanation applies here; the word is used exclusively to refer to the Name YHWH.</i> written in <i>Give to the Eternal, sons of the mighty</i><sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 29" href="/Psalms.29"><i>Ps</i>. 29</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ḥuna said: If it were said that they are seventeen, tell him the one against sectarians the Sages fixed already at Jabneh<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Ḥuna’s question is an attack against all who try to find a reason for the eighteen benedictions by pointing out that in the 500 years between the institution of formalized prayer by the Men of the Great Assembly and the Synhedrion of Rabban Gamliel, the Amidah prayer consisted of only 17 benedictions. Rebbi Ḥuna’s answer is that the eighteenth benediction also is an institution of the Synhedrion, the legal successor of the Great Assembly, and, therefore, all eighteen benedictions have the same status.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose objected before Rebbi Yose, but is it not written <i>the God of Glory thundered</i><sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 29:3" href="/Psalms.29.3"><i>Ps</i>. 29:3</a>. This Divine name is not an <i>askara</i>.</i>? He said to him: Has it not been stated: “He takes together the one against sectarians and sinners in ‘He Who subdues offenders’, the one for elders and proselytes in ‘Assurance to the righteous’, and the one for David in ‘He Who builds Jerusalem.’<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">This implies that the benediction containing the curse upon apostates and sectarians (i. e., Christians) was not newly introduced at Jabneh but that it was a modification of an old benediction directed at those who wantonly transgress the commandments of the Torah. Hence, the original number of benedictions instituted by the Great Assembly was 18. The Palestinian version had no separate benediction asking for the return of the Davidic rulers. The Babylonian version has no mention of the Davidic dynasty in the prayer for the rebuilding of Jerusalem; it is retained in today’s Yemenite prayerbook. Current Ashkenazic and Sephardic versions combine the Palestinian prayer for Jerusalem and the Davidic dynasty in benediction 14, with the Babylonian one for the Davidic dynasty alone in benediction 15. Hence, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 29" href="/Psalms.29">Psalm 29</a> accommodates both the Galilean 18 benedictions based on the number of mentions of the Tetragrammaton, and the 19 of the Babylonian version, based on all invocations of the Deity in the Psalm.</i>” Then you have enough to have ˂a Divine Name˃<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Added from G and <b>ב</b>.</i> for each one of them.
Rebbi Ḥanania in the name of Rebbi Phineas: Corresponding to the eighteen times the Patriarchs are mentioned together in the Torah, Abraham, Isaac, Jacob. If somebody will tell you that there are nineteen, tell him that <i>Behold, the Eternal was standing on it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 23:13" href="/Genesis.23.13"><i>Gen</i>. 23:13</a>. In this verse Jacob is not mentioned by name.</i> is not counted. If somebody will tell you that there are seventeen, tell him that <i>My name should be called over them, as well the names of my fathers Abraham and Isaac</i><sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 48:16" href="/Genesis.48.16"><i>Gen</i>. 48:16</a>. Jacob’s name is mentioned implicitly.</i> is counted.
Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: Corresponding to the eighteen times “commandment” is mentioned in the second description of the Tabernacle<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The description of the actual building of the Tabernacle.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada said, only from <i>And with him Aholiab ben Aḥisamakh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex</i>. 38:32. From the start of the section, v. 21, there would be 19 times mention of <i>the Eternal commanded to Moses.</i></i> to the end of the book.
<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">These paragraphs about the number of benedictions in the <i>Amidah</i> of special days are simply copied from <i>Berakhot</i>; they have no relevance here since fasting is forbidden on Sabbath and holidays. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 29a" href="/Berakhot.29a">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 29a</a>.</i> The seven of Sabbath, from where? Rebbi Isaac said, corresponding to the seven <i>voices</i> written in <i>Give to the Eternal, sons of the mighty</i><sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 29" href="/Psalms.29"><i>Ps</i>. 29</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yudan Antodraya said, corresponding to the seven Divine Names in <i>Psalm, Song for the Sabbath Day</i><sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 92" href="/Psalms.92"><i>Ps</i>. 92</a>.</i>.
Nine of New Year’s Day, from where? Rebbi Abba from Carthage said: Corresponding to the nine Divine Names in Hanna’s prayer; there it is written at the end: <i>The Eternal will judge the ends of the earth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 2:10" href="/I_Samuel.2.10"><i>1Sam</i>. 2:10</a>.</i>.
The twenty-four of fast days, from where? Rebbi Ḥelbo and Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman both say, corresponding to the twenty-four times that entreaty, prayer, and supplication are mentioned in Solomon’s prayer<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 8" href="/I_Kings.8"><i>1K</i>. 8</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 29a" href="/Taanit.29a">Babli 29a</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of (Rebbi) &lt;rav&gt;<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Since Rebbi Jeremiah was a student of R. Ze`ira, the reading Rav of the parallel sources has to be accepted.</i> Jeremiah, a private person has to mention the occasion on a public fast day. Where does he say it? Between “Redeemer of Israel” and “Healer of the sick.<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Between the Seventh and Eighth benedictions, as a separate benediction.</i>” What does he say? “Answer us, Eternal, answer us in this time and season because we are in great trouble, do not hide Your presence from us, do not be oblivious to our supplications, because You, Eternal, will answer in time of trouble, redeem and save in any time of distress. <i>They cried to the Eternal in their trouble, He led them out from their distress</i><sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 107:6" href="/Psalms.107.6"><i>Ps</i>. 107:6</a> conflated with v. 13.</i>. Praise to You, Eternal, Answering in times of trouble.” Rebbi Yannai ben Rebbi Ismael in the name &lt;of the house of rebbi yannai says: in “he who hears prayer.”<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The Fifteenth benediction, place of all personal additions to the weekday prayers.</i>The Fifteenth benediction, place of all personal additions to the weekday prayers. &lt;Rebbi Jonah&gt;<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Part of the text, added from G, missing here by scribal error because of homeoteleuton.</i> in the name of (Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish) &lt;rav&gt;<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to decide between the two readings since it is unusual that a very late Amora should quote directly an Amora of the first or second generation.</i>: Even an individual who decreed a fast day on himself has to mention the occasion. Where does he say it? Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rav Ḥuna: Similar to Friday night and Sabbath day<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">During every prayer he has to add the special prayer formulated for fast days.</i>. Rebbi Mana said: I, who did not check whether one follows Rav Jeremiah or Rebbi Yannai the son of Rebbi Ismael, went to the study hall and I heard: “Rav Ḥuna in the name of Rav: Even an individual who decreed a fast day on himself has to mention the occasion.” Rebbi Yose objected that a <i>baraita</i> disagrees: Every day one prays eighteen benedictions, including the night after Sabbath and the nights after Yom Kippur and after Public Fasts. Since Rebbi Yose said, this <i>baraita</i> disagrees, it contradicts the statement: between “Redeemer of Israel” and “Healer of the sick.<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Mana’s argument goes as follows: Since R. Yose stated that the <i>baraita</i> contradicts Rav, he takes the <i>baraita</i> to mean that a private person always recites 18 benedictions on weekdays, never 19, even on fast days. The additional days are mentioned to point out that insertions for a private person are always accommodated in an existing benediction, never as an additional one, in contrast to the opinion of R. Aqiba who states in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 5:2:2-16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.5.2.2-16"><i>Berkahot</i>5:2</a> that <i>Havdalah</i> Saturday nights is a separate benediction. In addition, it is stated that on those days when the additional <i>Ne`ilah</i> prayer is recited, the Day of Atonement and fast days for a severe draught, the <i>Ne`ilah</i> prayer does not replace the following night’s prayer as discussed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 4:1:2-33" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.4.1.2-33">Halakhah <i>Berakhot</i>4:1</a>. The Yerushalmi does not address the question what the reader says on a public fast day which is not a fast for rain; the Babli decides that the reader will recite עננו as a separate benediction between “Redeemer of Israel” and “Healer of the sick.” This decision does not go against R. Mani’s argument.</i>”
Rebbi Aḥa bar Isaac in the name of the older Rebbi Huna of Sepphoris: An individual must mention the occasion on the Ninth of Av. What does he say? “Have mercy, Eternal, our God, in Your great mercy and your trusted kindness, towards us, Your people Israel, Your city Jerusalem, Zion the dwelling place of Your glory, and on the mourning, destroyed, and desolate city that is given into the hand of strangers, trampled down by haughty peoples, that was raped by legions and desecrated by idol worshippers, when You had given her to Your people Israel in love as property, and let her be inherited by the seed of Yeshurun, for in fire You destroyed her and in fire You will build her in the future as it has been said: <i>But I shall be for her, says the Eternal, a wall of fire around, and Glory I shall be in her midst</i>.”<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Zechariah 2:9" href="/Zechariah.2.9"><i>Zach</i>. 2:9</a>. This is the only talmudic text of this prayer, reproduced in R. Saadia’s <i>siddur</i>. However all current prayer rites have another text, given by Maimonides.</i>
Rebbi Eudaimon of Sepphoris asked before Rebbi Mana: Where does one say this? He said to him: You still do not understand? So says Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: Anything for the future one says in “Service”<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">The prayer for the restoration of the Temple service, benediction 16, Babylonian 17.</i> and anything about the past one says in “Thanksgiving”<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Benediction 17, Babylonian 18. However all known prayer manuals, starting with R. Saadia, treat this text as an alternative for the prayer for Jerusalem, benediction 14.</i>. The Mishnah says as much<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 9:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.9.4.1">Mishnah <i>Berakhot</i>9:5</a>.</i>: “One gives thanks for the past and cries about the future.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 4:3:2-4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.4.3.2-4.5"><i>Berakhot</i> 4:3</a> where is its logical place; it has no relation with the topics discussed in <i>Ta`aniot</i>. The topic is a shortened form ofthe <i>Amidah</i> available as private prayer for people who either are ignorant or are inhibited by the circumstances from reciting the entire prayer. The short form certainly is inadmissible if an addition for public or voluntary fast day is required.</i> What are “seven similar to eighteen”? Rav says the end of each benediction and Samuel says the start of each benediction. Some formulate “seven similar to eighteen” and some formulate “eighteen similar to eighteen.” He who says “seven similar to eighteen” supports Samuel, he who says “eighteen similar to eighteen” supports Rav. Rebbi Ze`ira sent Rebbi Naḥum to Rebbi Yannai ben Rebbi Ismael and said to him: What are the “seven similar to eighteen” of Samuel? He said to him: “Give us understanding, have pleasure in our repentance, forgive us, redeem us, heal our sicknesses, bless our years; Rebbi Ḥaggai said, in the rainy season one says: with blessed rains, in the season of dew one says: with blessed dew; for You gather in the dispersed, Yours it is to judge the misguided, put down Your hand on the wicked, but in You may rejoice all who hope for You to rebuild Your city, to renew Your Temple, because before we call You will answer as it is said: <i>It shall be before they call that I shall answer, they still shall be talking while I will listen</i><sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 65:24" href="/Isaiah.65.24"><i>Is</i>. 65:24</a>.</i>. <i>Praised be the Eternal, Who certainly hears the voice of my supplications</i><sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 28:6" href="/Psalms.28.6"><i>Ps.</i>28:6</a>.</i>. Praise to You, Eternal, Who hears prayer.” And he says the first three and the last three benedictions.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Jehudah says, it was not necessary to recite <i>Zikhronot</i> and <i>Shofarot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The two additional benedictions in the <i>musaf</i> prayer of New Year’s Day; each one accompanied by the blowing of the <i>shofar</i>. The text of each addition is followed by an invocation and a doxology as spelled out in Mishnaiot 4–10.</i>, but he recites in their stead, <i>if famine will be in the land, if a plague will be in the land</i><sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 8:37" href="/I_Kings.8.37"><i>1K</i>. 8:37</a> ff.</i>, etc. <i>The Eternal’s Word which was to Jeremiah in the matter of droughts</i><sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 14:1" href="/Jeremiah.14.1"><i>Jer</i>. 14:1</a> ff.</i>. And he recites their conclusions<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">The final sentence, “He Who heard …” and the following doxology as explained in Mishnaiot 4–10.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:5:23" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.5.23"><i>Berakhot</i>1:8 (Notes 281–285</a>, <b>ב</b>). The corrector’s addition and changes, given in brackets, are unsupported by the parallel sources and have to be deleted.</i> All benedictions after their seals; one does not say a verse as [after a] benediction<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The “seal” of a longer benediction is the last sentence “Praise to You, Eternal, ..” It is stated that this seal is what makes the benediction valid and it cannot be a Biblical verse.</i>. Rebbi Isaac (bar) &lt;ben rebbi&gt; Eleazar objected before Rebbi Yose: Because you say after their seals, one does not say a verse as benediction<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">Since the two statements appear together in one <i>baraita</i>, there should be a logical connection between them but there seems to be none.</i>? They said, (that young man is intelligent,) [so is the matter] because he thinks what is the meaning of “after their seals”? It is that when he was standing in the morning prayer and mentioned the text of evening prayers but he caught himself and finished with the text of morning prayers, then he did his duty<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">“They” are the members of R. Yose’s Academy. They do not disagree with the rule which they state, but they note that the <i>baraita</i> does not deal with it; its correct interpretation is by R. Aḥa.<br>About the rule itself, there is disagreement between the Yerushalmi and the parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 12a" href="/Berakhot.12a">Babli, <i>Berakhot</i>12a</a>, where it is stated: “If in the morning one started with ‘Creator of light’ and ended with ‘Making evenings dark’, he did not do his duty. If in the evening he starts with `He Who makes evenings dark’ and ends with `Creator of light’, he did not do his duty. But if he started with ‘Creator of light’ and ended with ‘Making evenings dark’, he did his duty. The principle is: Everything is determined by the seal.” The Babli asserts that if in evening prayers one started out wrongly with the text appropriate for morning prayers then everything is O.K. if only the final doxology is correct. But the Yerushalmi speaks of one “who is standing in morning prayers and mentioned evening”, i. e., he started out correctly, forgot himself in the middle, and remembered in time to close with the correct text. The principles of prayer texts were instituted by the Men of the Great Assembly. They determined the contents of the prayers but not, in general, their texts. (For the Amidah, a later Mishnah reports that Rebbi Eliezer holds that one who repeats the same text for more than 30 days cannot really be an honest supplicant).
